	<review>
		<date>2012-04-27</date>
		<username>april-kobus</username>
		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>Leaves all that watch a sense of urgency to stand up for what they believe in</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The Help, based off of the 2009 novel by Kathryn Stockett, tells a story of three courageous women who join together to uncover ways they are mistreated as African American housemaids in the 1960's. Taking place in Jackson, Mississippi, these types of actions were unheard of. If caught, the members of "the help" would face arrest, never be able to find work again, and be shun from the city, however this did not stop them telling their story. Eugenia "Skeeter" Phelan, played by Emma Stone, has just graduated from college and come back to her hometown. After scoring her first job as a cleaning advice columnist which she is clueless about, she turns to Aibileen (Viola Davis) who is the African American housemaid of an old old childhood friend. to ask for advice. In doing so, she begins to notice how poorly Aibileen and other members of the help are treated, inspiring her to write a novel though the eyes of the help. Skeeter knows the dangers this can cause for Aibileen, ignores the consequences. After much convincing, Aibileen agrees to help, eventually recruiting her best friend Minny (Octivia Spencer) and other maids to join in the fight against the cities unjust treatment of the help. The writing of this book soon becomes much more than any of the women involve could have imagined, giving each of the maids a voice as well as a chance to finally tell the world what was seldom spoken of. It transforms into an important movement that was able to change the entire city of Jackson, Mississippi and the lives of all housemaids around the state. The theme of this film focuses on racism and courage. Racism is unfortunately the more serious of the two, as it brings many back to the cruel and shameful acts placed on innocent African Americans. The film steers away from much of the hate crimes happening in the 60's, yet still shows how racism had an impact in a more subtle way. Throughout the film, the town is adamant on the idea of creating separate bathrooms in the homes of those with maids. Many scenes show Aibileen and Minny sneaking around to simply use an inside bathroom or being forced to use separate outhouses. Skeeter was one of the few who saw the wrong in this and to a chance to make a change.In a more positive light, "The Help" brings attention to the moral courage of the African American women of Mississippi. Throughout the film, Aibileen is shown patience and kindness to the most racist white families, raising their children to have respect for all people, including themselves. She works hard to make them understand their worth in the world often repeating kind works of encouragement, "you is kind, you is smart, you is important". Beyond everything else, Aibileen, Milly, and other maids show courage as they tell their stories to Skeeter, risking their freedom, their jobs, and their families, all for the sake of future generations. The movie The Help shows a true testament to the courage of the women of the past, and the limits they were willing to reach in order to stand up for what they believed in. Women, just as those portrayed in the film, have paved the way to our future. This well written storyline sends a positive message to all generations, leaving all that watch a sense of urgency to stand up for what they believe in and make a difference in what's wrong in the world.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>julian-mumford</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>Fine acting and a great story make this a recommended watch</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Based on the best selling book of the same name by first time novelist Kathryn Stockett, we are transported back to a lightly fictionalized Mississippi circa 1960.This is a time when folks were either white or coloured, simplistically whites had the money, privilege and law on their side and used that power however they goddam pleased. Of course, rich white folk would need "Help" running their large houses and bringing up their children whilst they pursued more worthwhile pursuits, like Bridge and gossip.The story is fictional and focuses on a group of rich white ladies and their "negro" maids, Abileen (Viola Davis) and Minny (Octavia Spencer - Oscar winning for this role). To counterbalance the institutionalized and accepted racism clearly on view, we have Skeeter (Emma Stone), who whilst not totally convinced, is at least asking the question why everything is like it is. Skeeter acts as the modern audiences window into this strange and rapidly changing world.Skeeter is an aspiring reporter gently teasing out a story from the reluctant maids, in the hope of producing a book detailing what the "Help" really thinks of their life, work and those employing them. There are huge and life threatening risks for those involved in helping to write the book. Starvation, trumped up theft charges and worse, are all real possibilities in a world that Skeeter can never truly enter.Some employers look after their staff with respect and dignity over many years, treating them as family. However, seemingly the younger generation, do not. For many, the line between slave and employee is a fine one indeed, lives blighted or destroyed at the passing whim of a rich woman's choosing.Notable characters are Celia, ostracized and desperate to be accepted by the "in crowd" yet doomed to fail, Hilly (Howard), a vicious and small minded woman and the easily influenced Leefolt (O'Reilly). Skeeters mum (Janney) is a good woman but is tied to the past, despite her own humanity and intelligence desperately chafing at the conventions of the time.This is a film that has been embraced by book clubs, schools and history teachers and it is easy to see why. The acting is uniformly good, especially Davis and Spencer who bring a quiet dignity and respect their roles, acting as representatives for all those that had no voice at the time. Davis whose brief turn in "Doubt" with Meryl Streep, again proves her acting ability.Perhaps the most shocking realization is that this is not ancient history, such events are only fifty years ago. Separate bathrooms for "them coloreds", who may bring bring different diseases to the house and yet can be trusted with the families supposed prize possessions, their children.The film is uplifting, funny and poignant in parts although not as hard hitting as the book and does on occasion appear somewhat "airbrushed" to avoid offending current sensibilities. Whilst not a Hollywood ending, loose ends are tied better on the screen and justice seen to be done, the book exists in a more realistic shade of grey.There is much to enjoy and much to be ashamed of but overall this is film that deserves to be seen and discussed. We are never too far away from such events to take the freedoms, acceptance and tolerance that most of us enjoy, for granted.Summary A film that any audience can enjoy, serious, well intentioned albeit somewhat sanitised version of the book which in itself perhaps only scratches the surface of what life might have been like for those in service.Fine acting and a great story make this a recommended watchhttp://julesmoviereviews.blogspot.co.nz/</fullReview>
	</review>

I think this movie should be required viewing in schools, not only in the USA, but all over the world. I couldn't find any flaws whatsoever in this admirable production, technical or otherwise. A perfect movie in every sense, extraordinary actors, superb director.</fullReview>

		<username>TheFamilyBerzurcher</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Will It Help?</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>THE HELP has proved critically divisive and rightfully so. When it gets it right, it's marvelous. When it gets it wrong, it's shameful. Still, a magnificent group of actresses render this film as sweet as chocolate pie -- made not by Octavia Spencer.The first decisive critical split comes in the characters. Just like so many nostalgic period works we see, writers forget to develop characters - only archetypes. These women are divided into two groups, White Racist Hussies and Color-Free Saintly Perfections. There is Black, there is White, but there is no Gray in THE HELP. Morality is so sadly made into an on/off switch any three-year old can figure out. In dealing with an issue still so pungent as American racism, Tate Taylor (writer-director) should know that things are much more complex than are presented here. However, so much praise is due to Octavia Spencer and Viola Davis. Their individual performances easily stand alone, but in tandem they create a tangible energy and ambition. Moments they share provide some hope for contemporary cinema. Emma Stone is also great. I hesitate in evaluating her because she hesitates in her own role. She is so well-crafted for modernity and self-referential comedy. Visibly, she was working hard to adapt to the period style. It paid off. The rest of the company does as well as possible in their stereotyped restrictions with special applause for Jessica Chastain as Celia. It's a long way from THE TREE OF LIFE, but here she brings to life THE HELP's most vivid and realized character.The photography is fine. The colors look like a children's book and some of the bright hues do breathe life into an otherwise deadened 60's suburbia. Sound, design, make-up, and costumes all do well enough jobs that I don't feel like complaining and I can't find a reason to commend. Not so in the editing room. The picture is long. Towards the end, it isn't bothersome at all. However, the opening of the film could have lost five to ten minutes of footage as introductions drag on and on. Sure enough, rewatching the first half-hour uncovers one unnecessary scene and two that are egregiously long. Also, the final confrontation between Aibileen and Hilly seems an appendix. It does nothing for the film but offer one a chance to shout at the other. As if Taylor were trying to tie up the film with a nice bow regardless of the fact that Aibileen had just lost her job. Here again, THE HELP ends on a strangely certain note. Civil Rights was only a dream in 1964; the movement only beginning to gain speed. If there was a happy ending for Aibileen, it wasn't at the denouement of THE HELP.Disregarding the archetypes, the bad coda, and some numbness, THE HELP did beautifully conjure age-old imagery of White kids being raised by Black women. It's one that so many of our parents remember. Was that a way to improve race relations? Were those awful days polishing silver the seeds of a great improvement? THE HELP might mean many things here. It might mean Skeeter writing a revolutionary book. It might mean Hilly "helping" herself to a nice slice of pie. It might mean Aibileen raising children while hers is at home. The point is that help needs to come from everywhere "and it needs to start with the truth," as they say. Black ladies will raise the kids. Black ladies write the books. Black ladies clean the kitchens. Black ladies write the newspaper columns.But the most unfortunate flaw in the film is that a White lady saves the Black ladies. Perpetuating the thankless circumstances that need to end.74.5</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Well Rounded and Enjoyable</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>"The Help", critically and commercially praised as "extraordinary" and "sensational", was a film that I felt must be overrated. A film that makes women proud to be where they are, who they are and gives us all that little bit of courage we were missing previously.The only facet of this deliciously well-composed film that fell-flat was the "romantic-interest" of Stone's character Skeeter. 		<date>2012-02-24</date>
		<username>seaview1</username>
		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>The HELP and the Women Behind the Scenes</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Based on the best seller by Kathryn Stockett and adapted and directed by her childhood friend Tate Taylor, The Help is a personal film that is affecting in its depiction of social injustice in the treatment of black females who served as nannies and maids in the Old South during the turbulent 1960's. As an ensemble piece, it features a host of memorable, Oscar caliber performances.A black female, Aibileen (Viola Davis), in Jackson, Mississippi, during the 1960's relates a story of life during that period where white families relied on black housekeepers to cook, clean and even raise their white children. These housekeepers in effect became second mothers to these children and did so for generations. Aibileen works in one such household, as does her combative friend, Minny (Octavia Spencer), who works for the stern Hilly (grandly played for nastiness by Bryce Dallas Howard). Fresh from college, Skeeter (Emma Stone) has a reunion of sorts with her old friends (including Hilly) from the community. Absent is the local outcast, Celia (Jessica Chastain), who is shunned by the group. Dreaming of being a writer, Skeeter has been curious about the life of these house servants and the injustices and humiliating treatment they must face that border on outright racism that has been passed down from family to family. Even as a child, Skeeter has fond memories of her own housekeeper Constantine (Cicely Tyson) who left one day under mysterious circumstances. Encouraged by her publisher, she decides to write a book based on the experiences of these servants. Under the fear of being discovered and fired (or worse), one by one these black women come forth to tell their stories. When civil rights activist, Medgar Evers, is murdered, it creates a firestorm of violence as these women risk everything to get the truth in print, and the results will forever alter their lives.As a chronicle of a lesser known civil rights issue and injustice, this film offers some interesting insights into a modern form discrimination. While most of the white community is portrayed as villains and almost caricatures, perhaps that's just the point: that such a backwards culture existed not that long ago. There are a number of subplots including the attempts to set up Skeeter on a blind date and Celia's awkward attempts hide her domestic inadequacies from her husband by hiring a maid. Then there is the fate of Skeeter's Constantine that is heartbreaking.As much as the acting is superior across the board, the film does not quite work as a whole as well as one would hope. The narrative seems a bit uneven so things don't flow smoothly from scene to scene. It's the only thing that keeps the film from being even better than it is. In a way the parts or scenes are better than the whole, and many of those scenes are quite good and authentic. Despite its serious subject, the film does have its humorous moments especially in the infamous confrontation between Minny and Hilly where justice is served! It is the highlight of the film.There are any number of performances that shine particularly Davis (a standout), Spencer, Howard, Chastain, and Stone. Even Sissy Spacek has a witty, offbeat role as an elderly mom at odds with her daughter Hilly. But it's the film's moments that will stay with you; they buoy The Help into our hearts.</fullReview>
	</review>

The movie explains the relationships between African American maids during the 1960s and their families they provided for. With constant care taking, daily routines and chores many of the maids went about their lives saying very few words to the white folks. When Miss Skeeter Phelan, a University grad with a passion for writing, comes back to town, she stirs up a hot pot of drama. Persuading the maids to tell-all about their unspoken stories and redemption towards white families they have worked for, may have sounded like a good idea at the time. Lets just say that some thoughts are better off not spoken. The passion and friendship Skeeter shows towards these women is truly touching and heart-warming. This movie shows how the determination of one person can change the lives of many. At some point everyone is forced to make the choice of either staying with or breaking the status quo. The light humor and strong Southern accents will definitely keep you intrigued throughout the entire movie. The Help is a must see.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>'THE HELP' could have used some help in polishing out a good film.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>'THE HELP': Three and a Half Stars (Out of Five) Another 2011 Academy Award 'Best Picture' nominee, this one being financially the most successful of the bunch. It's an unbalanced film combining serious emotional drama dealing with real racial issues with exaggerated sentimentality and cheesy comic relief. It was written and directed by actor turned filmmaker Tate Taylor, which he adapted from a book (of the same name) by Kathryn Stockett. It's an ensemble piece in which three actresses were also nominated for Academy Awards (Viola Davis, Octavia Spencer and Jessica Chastain).Emma Stone stars as Skeeter Phelan, an author in 1960's Mississippi who recently graduated college and moved back home with her family. She discovers that the maid who raised her (Cicely Tyson) has quit but is suspicious about what really happened to her and can't get much information out of her parents (Allison Janney and Brian Kerwin). After witnessing more abuse of other maids she's come to know in the area she decides to write a book from the African American maids point of view about working for white families. At first many are afraid to help her, out of fear what could result from it, but she slowly earns the help of one bold maid name Aibillen Clark (Davis).The movie is hard hitting at times but other times the drama feels far too forced. Still it's much better than the likes of something like 'THE BLIND SIDE'. It's also filled with odd comedy including those involving fecal matter. None of that quite comes off right but when the film works it really works and is very memorable at times. Davis is outstanding in the lead and Chastain and Bryce Dallas Howard (the two would be sisters) are both exceptional in supporting roles as well. I found Spencer's character to be too much of an exaggerated stereotype forced to deliver the bulk of the bad comedy, which Spencer does a good job at but not an Oscar worthy performance (due to the directing and writing of her role). The movie as a whole is a mixed bag but definitely worth a look.Watch our review show 'MOVIE TALK' at: http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=Sd0S1srQ9T8</fullReview>

		<username>evanston_dad</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>Far from Perfect But Carries a Welcome Message</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>"The Help" has so many positive things to say and is full of such good actors giving such good performances that it's pretty hard to outright dislike. It's one of those movies that seems really good while you're in the middle of watching it but begins to fall apart somewhat when you think back on it. I wonder how much different the viewing experience would be if you'd read the book on which it was based (I have not). As it is, there are many parts that feel like they're in the film because they're in the book but weren't as fully fleshed out in the movie as I imagine they were in the novel. An entire storyline involving Jessica Chastain as a helpless housewife who turns to her black maid to help her is welcome because it gives Chastain a chance to deliver a winning and immensely likable performance, but I'm not sure what it really had to do with the rest of the movie around it. The character played by Allison Janney doesn't make much sense at all. Representative of a certain kind of white person who's basically well meaning but allows peer pressure to turn her eyes away from the injustices of segregation, her character is first portrayed as a foolish and vain woman with whom we're supposed to be disgusted and then turns on a dime to become a champion for her daughter, played as appealingly as always by Emma Stone, when her daughter writes an expose of white oppression from the point of view of the black household staffs. And don't even get me started on the character played by Bryce Dallas Howard, an unlikable actress anyway who's given a gross caricature to play, so vile but ultimately so juvenile that she threatens to undermine the entire point of the movie -- if all racists were as silly and as easy to dismiss as her, all of our race problems would be solved.Viola Davis and Octavia Spencer, as the two black maids at the center of the story, deserve the accolades that have been directed their way. My only complaint, which is really a compliment, is that Davis had been given more screen time.Grade: B+</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>meeza (themeezaman@hotmail.com)</username>
		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>The Help gets the job done!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>"The Help" needs no help in reaching for more success. It has captured the hearts of critics and moviegoers alike; it has been nominated for a few Academy Awards and was a surprise box-office hit. Writer-Director Tate Taylor's film about an aspiring Mississippi writer in the 60's who decides to write a book detailing the lives of African-American maids through their point of views did sure help uplift my spirits upon watching it. I thought it was a sensational, inspirational movie that was "maid to love". Taylor's tailor-made script and direction helped make it one of the best movies of the year, but the Academy sure needs some help because they snubbed Taylor for both director and screenplay Oscar nominations. Emma Stone stars as Skeeter Phelan, the go-getter young white Southern writer who gets a gig writing a laundry column in a Jackson, Mississippi newspaper; that was the gateway that brought her the idea on having African-American maids detail their challenging experiences as maids in Southern homes of wealthy, white families that even had segregated bathrooms built for the help. Viola Davis also stars as Aibileen Clark, an African-American melancholy maid who eventually decides to spill the maid beans to Skeeter. Octavia Spencer co-stars as Minny Jackson, the vociferous maid who is no Minnie Mouse when it comes to proving her points, whatever that means. All of these performances from the Big Acting Three of "The Help" (Stone, Davis, and Spencer) were remarkable and Oscar-worthy. Davis and Spencer both received Oscar nominations for their grand performances, but I think the Academy was stoned by snubbing Stone. With Spencer as the frontrunner for a Best Supporting Actress win, Octavia will soon be a "Spencer: For Hire" by being offered lucrative roles in major future productions. Other stellar supporting acting performances which helped "The Help" have one of the best acting ensembles of the year come from Allison Janney as Skeeter's sick mother who pressures Skeeter to get married, Bryce Dallas Howard as the racist villain Hilly Holbrook who is simply pure evil, and Jessica Chastain with her Oscar-nominated performance as the scene-stealing southern socialite Celia Foote. How could I have chastised Chastain by not including her in the aforementioned Big Acting Three of "The Help"! So, let's call it the Fab Acting Four of "The Help" with all apologies to Janney, Howard, and the rest of the solid cast of thespians in this movie. There are some touching scenes of "The Help" that will help for you to have your weepie-hankie handy when seeing those heart-tugging moments. So help me help you and let me recommend for you to seek "The Help". ***** Excellent</fullReview>

		<date>2012-01-27</date>
		<username>MovieCriticDave</username>
		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>An entirely different way to explain "civil rights"</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Rarely does a movie so thoroughly immerse you in its story that you find yourself truly lost in the epic, captured by the soul of a tale that causes the deepest kind of introspections into your own morays, or a stripping away of the naiveté that may have created them. So it is with "The Help," a simply masterful tale that in its epic understatement creates a movie that could fundamentally recharacterize how we view the civil rights movement. "The Help," adapted from the Katherine Hockett novel of the same name, is a fictionalized story of Skeeter Phelan (Emma Stone), a young deep- South author seeking to tell the tale of the lives of black maids and nannies in mid-1960's Jackson, Mississippi through their eyes, and in their words.In telling the humble, and often humiliating, tales these women offer, "The Help" becomes a civil rights movie of an entirely different kind, one seen not in the form of protest and division, but one of man's inhumanity to man. "The Help" confronts the viewer with a reality our sensibilities reject, yet compels us to acknowledge. Viola Davis stars as Aibileen Clark, the maid first approached by Skeeter with the shocking proposal that she discuss her life as the primary caregiver to children in white households. Davis' performance is astonishingly and brilliantly on-the-mark, richly earning her Oscar nomination for best actress. Clark's role is vital in the film's exposition, because if we do not accept her as the conduit through which the stories of other maids are delivered, the movie fails; yet Davis' portrayal of Clark as the sublimely humble heroine confronted to act with the courage of her faith resonates perfectly in the unfolding story. Clark's own story leads us to her friend Minny Jackson, played superbly by Octavia Spencer. Spencer's precision delivery of Jackson's wit and skepticism is the perfect contrast to the humble and understated Aibileen, and forces us to make sure we trust Skeeter's motives in writing her book. "The Help" is a richly woven story of still-persistent attitudes towards black Americans in the deep south; that we hear Clark narrate passages from Mississippi law governing the "treatment" of "coloreds" astonishes the viewer all the more, realizing that while the story may be fictionalized, their legally institutionalized treatment was not. Those attitudes of race hatred are rolled masterfully into the manifestly detestable character of Hilly Holbrook (Bryce Dallas Howard), the socialite in charge of the local junior league, and self-appointed leader of the response to the "colored issue." "The Help" isn't perfect - it comes perilously close to weaving too many characters into too short a time, and at that the movie rings in at a plump 2:26, even after some apparent harsh last-minute editing. Side stories from the book involving a senator's son, Skeeter's mother's ill health, Skeeter's nanny Constantine played by Cicely Tyson, to name only a few, are incorporated, but abbreviated or changed substantially. Fortunately, the underlying story is so strong that the film doesn't become fragmented or suffer as a result. Many movies are characterized as an experience for their special effects , grand cinematography, or epic scale. "The Help" is just such an experience, but for none of those reasons. It is the compelling performances of a tremendous cast that draw out the tremendous story of real people fighting real prejudice in a region that sees that prejudice as perfectly normal and acceptable. That "The Help" compels the viewer to live in their shoes, if but for a brief time in the abstraction of a movie theater, is a monument to its greatness."The Help," in and of itself, is a fictional tale. Yet the masterful way its fiction reveals and explains its underlying truth, represents a simply brilliant piece of movie-making.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Watch it More for the Actresses, than the Storytelling</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>"The Help" is about black women being employed as maids and nannies in Jackson, Mississippi back in the racially-tense early 1960s. Skeeter Phelan (Emma Stone) is an independent tomboyish young lady who could not help but notice how badly her bridge buddies were treating their black maids. This terrible racist attitude was most exemplified by the pretty but contemptible Hilly Holbrook (Bryce Dallas Howard), who was not even shy about her nasty behavior. Skeeter then secretly interviews maids Aibilene Clark (a serious Viola Davis) and Minny Jackson (a sassy Octavia Spencer), who bravely tell Skeeter their stories of oppression in white homes. How will the town react when Skeeter's book finally gets published?The movie is another example of excellent ensemble acting. Viola Davis and Octavia Spencer have the showiest roles. Spencer, in particular, figures in the most memorable scene about how her Milly character gets back at Hilly using a pie. Emma Stone is also quite good as the modern thinking Skeeter. Jessica Chastain sparkles as Celia Foote, a naive housewife from the poor side of town who was ostracized by Hilly and her friends. The more minor characters of Sissy Spacek (as Hilly's mom) and Allison Janney (as Skeeter's mom) also make their mark despite their shorter screen time. Cicely Tyson has a memorable moment in her brief and silent screen appearance as Skeeter's nanny.The treatment of the serious story is light, with just the right amount of humor. It is a Disney production so we do not really expect this to be "Roots" or "A Color Purple." The subject matter in itself may not really appeal too much for all audiences especially in this part of the world, but the message of hope and redemption will make this movie universally meaningful.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>A satisfying film with superb acting</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>So I finally watched "The Help", a movie which, considering the precursors, has good chances of earning a considerable number of Oscar nominations next Tuesday. And it sure didn't disappoint me.The story revolves around the society of Jackson, Mississippi, in the 60's, focusing on the relationship between the black maids and the white women they work for. There's Skeeter (Emma Stone), a modern young woman who, unlike most of her lady friends, has graduated from University and has aspirations of becoming a writer. As a result of his job as a "homemaker hints" columnist in the local paper, for which she asks maid Aibileen (Viola Davis) for help, she decides to write a book about the Help.The film, hence, has a powerful message about tolerance and equality. I felt that, though this works well and gives the film depth, sometimes it gets a little too obvious and makes it falls into the sentimental, tear- jerking territory. It's not like it bothered me, because it didn't, but I just thought it could have been handled a little more subtly. But hey, Disney's involved in it, so what did we expect?The strong point of the film is the female cast. The actresses are perfect in their roles and they truly shine throughout the film. Emma Stone, as Skeeter, brings really well the modernity and the wit her character requires and Bryce Dallas Howard, though a little "cartoonish" sometimes as the evil and despicable Hilly Holbrook (which is understandable) really delivers too. But I found the following three performances even better: Viola Davis, as the suffered Aibileen, does great in the most heart- breaking scenes and shows once more what a good actress she is, after her short but top-notch role in Doubt, where she outshone everyone (even Meryl Streep!). In spite of this, I'm having a little problem about her being considered the lead for all the awards. Though she's one of the centers of the film, she is missing in a big part of it. Secondly, we got Octavia Spencer, as the sassy Minny Jackson. It's true that hers is not a difficult role to play, but she's really really funny and one of the best parts of the film.And last but not least, the wonderful Jessica Chastain. Her Celia Foote could have easily been played as an over-the-top, one-dimensional bimbo, but she brings a depth and humanity that make her character the most endearing and likable in the film, in my opinion. I enjoyed her scenes with Minny the best.Overall, "The Help" is a perfectly enjoyable film, with amazing performances and an interesting storyline. It doesn't get tiresome at all, despite its considerable length, so I really recommend it. Regarding the Oscars, though, I do not think it should win best picture, but I'd be happy if one of the ladies took home the award, which seems likely.</fullReview>

		<date>2012-01-22</date>
		<username>ElMaruecan82</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>Good script for a Great story, Great acting for Good characters ...</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>A woman-centered film about the treatment of black maids in Jackson, Mississippi during the 60's? the kind of pitches that instantly remind of "Mississippi Burning" and "The Color Purple", as if Tate Jackson's film, "The Help" was only begging for critical acclaim and Academy Awards. But if we were cynical enough to judge a movie only by its plot, we might also be able to judge people by the color of their skin, or their ethnic background... while there's so much more that justifies "The Help" success.Those who didn't have black maids as children, at least grew up with the immortal Pop-culture image of Hattie McDaniel in "Gone With the Wind" or the iconic Mammy-Two-Shoes in "Tom and Jerry" cartoons. I, myself, from a Moroccan background learned many stories about my Mom being raised in the 60's by a 'dada', which was the affectionate word used for black nannies, subtly masking the tragic reality of these women's historical backgrounds. My generation didn't have these 'dadas' but only maids from the country's poorer area, and the ways some were treated echo the film's saddest subplots. They were not all victims, as for my part, still have great memories about the maid who took care of me, whether it was cleaning me after 'I finished', or in the shower. My nanny stayed with us almost 20 years, and will forever be part of my greatest memories. Indeed, there are many maid stories and it's the combination of them that highlights the harsh reality of racism. The film is told from the perspective of Aibileen Clark, Viola Davis in one of this year's best performances as a veteran maid, interviewed by the heroine, Emma Stone as Skitter Phelan, an ambitious girl whose journalism rhymes (unfortunate cliché) with feminism. The film still doesn't fall in the convenient trap that would portray all the maids as victims but it features one of the most recently despicable villains: Blythe Dallas Howard as Hilly Holbrook, the authoritarian President of Jackson Junior League. Hilly embodies the ugliest part of racism, the one disguised with a condescending paternalism, mostly made from the sentiment of domination provided by wealth and education. Indeed, as much as many women were raised by black nannies in my country and in the film, most of them would never allow their daughter to marry an African man, except if he was rich and educated. The economical racism is still relevant today, especially if we dared to imagine, a rich black woman behaving like Hilly Holbrook with a Mexican maid. The treatment of racism in "The Help" is its greatest accomplishment, because we don't have burning crosses and Jackson is surprising Ku-Klux Klan-less ... although they felt necessary to include a scene of violence so random I wondered if it was really needed. I think the most thought-provoking aspect of the script was precisely in the way racism was depicted, something as banal as routine, and as natural as our most basic daily actions, which didn't make it less hateful. Toilets play a significant part in the film, sometimes much more than needed even for comedic purpose, but this perfectly shows that racism can be less about the glorification of a race than a constant reluctance toward one: refusing to touch someone or to sit on the same toilet... How many of us said that without being racist, were not attracted to a particular race, black, Chinese or even white? Some of us have precisely dealt with prejudice, and not necessarily as victims and the film mirrors if not our racism but our guiltiest thoughts and unconscious capability to sort-of understand racism, which turns us into accomplices. In "The Help", many white characters are not racist, but they participate to it because of their incapability to resist peer pressure and display tolerance in front of people, on the top of them, Skitter's mother Charlotte, Allison Phaney proving her immense talent to play motherly figures in Best Picture potentials and Ahna O'Reilly as Elizabeth Lefoit the mother of the adorable little girl, mother by title since Aibileen Clark, her nanny, is obviously the only real mother figure. Many scenes features this ambiguity of behavior, provoking in our open-minded consciences frustrated reactions. Needless to say that Skitter does resist to this pressure, since she's the one who made the book possible but strangely, I thought her character was too stereotypical while the film should have elevated itself above those clichés. Some scenes sounded probably great on the paper, but on-screen, embarrassed me a little, especially a romance that started from nowhere, and as I expected, went nowhere (like in many all-female cast, male characters were underdeveloped) and some maids, Eugenia's nanny to name one, were too 'noble' not to remind of the good old' wise black person cliché. It's one thing to avoid one-dimensional villains in a film, but it's even better when you don't make the opposite mistake and make some people unbelievably good when the hardship they endured would have made them tougher. This is why the most interesting performances came from Octavia Spencer as Minny Jackson and Jessica Chastain as Celia Foote. Both demonstrated, not without humor though, that the rejection endured by women in the 60's wasn't only based on racism, and some of the greatest scenes feature either Minny or Celia, or both, and I wouldn't be surprised if they received Oscar nominations, with a win for Spencer."The Help" will probably garner many awards in 2012, but I wonder if it does justice to the novel it's adapted from, because its main flaws come from a script whose parts could have been more equally developed, avoiding some subplots and enriching others. It's still one of the best films of 2011, but it really feels like it took its original story and inspiring message for granted.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Compelling story about racial prejudices. It avoids sentimentality, and keeps our attention throughout the full 146 minutes</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I was a bit reluctant to book tickets for this movie, as I assumed a bittersweet story. This is mostly due to the ingeniously constructed script. Events initially seeming to leave an open end, are fulfilled later on to be seamlessly integrated in the plot. I cannot think of many suggestions to edit away redundant scenes, or to strike some of the participants.An enormous number of people appear throughout the film, all of them needed to make up the story. For some reason this did not overwhelm us viewers. It was always clear who-was-who and what their role was. One word of advice: skip reading the Plot Synopsis on IMDb for this movie. It certainly will leave you confused behind, by giving a needlessly fragmented view on the story line.The only problem I have with the story is that it not only covers black and white racial prejudices, but also is populated with black and white characters, figuratively speaking. It cannot be that all blacks in the movie are good, and that nearly all whites treat them badly. This one-dimensionality may emphasize the general tendency of the story, but it is unrealistic and uncalled for. Within these confines, all actors are believable and perform very well.The open ending does not leave us unsatisfied, rather the contrary. Maybe it is the best solution to wrap-up the story. You can expect that some of the main characters will face difficult times, in spite of the momentum in the positive direction that is caused by the book that they helped to make. We see an example near the end where one of the helps is fired for the wrong reasons. This sort of revolutionary collateral damage cannot always be avoided, neither in movies nor in the real world. Racial prejudices won't disappear overnight, and may even take a few generations to overcome.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Not perfect, but quite interesting</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Every time I watch a movie about racial oppression (either it is set during the slavery period, or the subsequent segregation, or the current combination of volatile cultures), I cannot avoid some suspicions about the authentic intentions from the screenplay. Due to the fact that the subject is controversial, it can be used as a basis of intelligent and provocative reflections...or it can simply be used as an easy trick to draw attention and provoke emotions on the spectator. Which one of both categories does the film The Help belong to? I honestly cannot assure it, because its tone changes with an alarming frequency, from predictable soap-opera clichés to genuinely honest drama with a deep emotional resonance. Nevertheless, I think that the balance of the film definitely falls on the positive side, mainly because of its excellent performances and a precise narrative which never looses the focus on the main message.The screenplay from The Help shows the classic traces of a "compressed" story, in which some important points feel hasty or superficial, while other elements occupy space without substantially contributing to the story. Ie that that is normal in book adaptations, and I am sure that screenwriter Tate Taylor (who was also the director) tried to preserve the best he could from author Kathryn Stockett's book. However, I would have liked to see the time spent on some elements (such as an useless love story) invested on developing better some of the very interesting characters who do not have enough time on scene. Another small folly comes from the funny moments, which occasionally feel excessively forced and predictable.However, that did not avoid me from liking The Help pretty much because of the brilliant performances and the generally well written screenplay. The interaction between the characters Minny and Aibileen is among the best elements from the film, and it does not leave any place for sentimental manipulation, because it did not need from any exaggeration in order to perfectly express the suffering from the characters and the catharsis of simply venting their painful memories and sad experiences. Even though The Help is not based on a true story, it feels like that because of the sincere performances and the realism of the situations depicted, which are unfortunately credible in the historical framework the movie is set.As for the actresses, Viola Davis and Octavia Spencer bring extraordinary works which are absolutely worthy of the acclamation they are both receiving. Even though Emma Stone feels a bit darkened by Davis and Spencer, that does not avoid her work from being solid. Oh, and I don't wanna forget Bryce Dallas Howard bringing a perfect performance as a villain simultaneously worthy of hate and sorrow. So, despite some tonal and narrative irregularities, The Help definitely deserves a recommendation as a very interesting experience.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Worthy but weaknesses</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Kathryn Stockett's book on which this film is based (originally turned down by 60 publishers) and co-writer and director Tate Taylor's movie itself have both been outstanding successes in the United States where this tale of prejudice and racism towards house maids in 1960s Mississippi has clearly resonated in a country which has moved on sufficiently to elect a black president but still exhibits discrimination against African-Americans in so many aspects of its society. It is unquestionably a worthy work, championing an underprivileged ethnicity, class and gender and providing a slew of challenging roles for female actors, both black (Viola Davis and Octavia Spencer) and white (Emma Stone, Allison Janney, Bryce Dallas Howard and Jessica Chastain).But as a film it lacks a strong narrative drive and pacing, while the characterisations are one-dimensional with only Janney's character having any real nuance or complexity. And the book and film have been criticised for representing a white heroine as liberator of the black helps instead of suggesting that they were capable of self-empowerment. In spite of these weaknesses, this is a distinctive and well-made movie that has something to say and should be seen and taken to heart.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Good movie, not great</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>¨If you can love your enemy, you already have victory.¨ The Help has received a lot of Oscar buzz and I wouldn't be surprised if it gets many nominations, but I was a little disappointed after watching the film since I had such high expectations for it. The Help is a good, solid movie, but it just didn't live up to all the hype for me. Racial and segregation films like this one have been made before with much greater effects. This film felt a bit light at times, and reminded me a lot of Fried Green Tomatoes. I can't help but think this film could've been much stronger considering the strong cast it had and the social issues it was pointing out. The film is based on Kathryn Stockett's novel of the same name, and it was adapted for the screenplay by director Tate Taylor himself. This is Taylor's second film, after having made a small film three years ago named Pretty Ugly People. Taylor, a relatively unknown has jumped to fame with his work in this movie, but considering the material I think a good director could've brought so much more to the story. In a way this seems to be a light drama which is touching a serious and dark period for many people. There are some funny moments, but the movie is really long and drags during some periods of time. I thought that the last half hour was probably the best part of the movie and you end up leaving the theater with a smile in your face. It's a good movie with some terrific performances, but I just was expecting so much more.The Help is a reference to the maids in Jackson, Mississippi during the 60's when racial discrimination and segregation was really strong. Civil rights movements had begun, but separation between whites and blacks was still very much an issue. The movie centers around several Southern society girls and the relationship they have with their maids. Skeeter (Emma Stone) returns home after college to take care of her sick mother (Allison Janney) and gets a job at a local newspaper writing housekeeping articles. Since she has no clue whatsoever about housekeeping she decides to interview the maids. Skeeter is sad to hear that the maid she grew up with and was like a mother to her has left to Chicago, but realizes there might be more to it than her mother is telling her. Skeeter is the only single girl since all her friends are already married and have children. Her best friend Hilly (Bryce Dallas Howard) is president of a local society and has come up with the idea that every family should have a special and separate bathroom for their maids. This of course, because of fear that they will transmit some sort of disease to their children. Hilly is very cruel towards her maid Minny (Octavia Spencer) and one day in a dispute fires her. Minny then finds a job working for a very nice lady named Celia Foote (Jessica Chastain) who's just arrived back in Jackson with her husband and live in a huge house. She is very disorganized, but Minny helps her get the house organized. Meanwhile, Skeeter realizes how terrible most maids are treated and decides she wants to write a book about the help, but written with the maids point of view. Skeeter begins interviewing her maid's best friend, Aibeleen (Viola Davis), who has had a lot of experience working with high society white families. Hesitant at first Aibeleen then begins opening up and convincing other maids to tell their stories to Skeeter and the result might shake things up for the residents in Jackson, Mississippi.The best thing about The Help without a doubt is the performance from the entire cast. Emma Stone does a great job as the lead character fighting for the rights of some of the maids which have been such a great help to her, but at the same time realizes that her friends will probably be upset with what she's doing. She still speaks what's on her mind and isn't afraid to point out the things that are wrong. She is very courageous. Viola Davis is just excellent and will probably receive an Oscar nomination. She also has a lead role in my opinion and she is just good in every movie she's in. She does a great job at repressing the anger she sometimes feels after suffering so much injustice. Her friend Minny, played by Octavia Spencer, doesn't do such a great job at repressing that anger and sometimes explodes. Spencer really surprised me in this film; she does a great job and absolutely nails her role. She will get a lot more job offers after this. It's hard to pick just one performance because everyone in this film does such an outstanding job. Jessica Chastain is excellent in this movie in her supporting role and perhaps gives the best performance of all. She is sweet and naive, but also feels broken due to her incapacity of bearing children. It's really sad considering she probably would be a lovable mother, unlike the rest of the society moms who can't even pick up their own kids and leave it all to the maids. I can't end this review without mentioning Bryce Dallas Howard who is just so good at being bad, mean, and cruel. She truly is scary in this movie and plays an excellent villain. The Help is a good movie, I just was expecting a little more from the story, but still go see it.http://estebueno10.blogspot.com/</fullReview>

		<username>Mike Stolyarov</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>A movie that everyone should see</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>There is no such thing as a "perfect movie" and "The Help" is not an exception from that rule. It, however, falls into the select category of "must see" movies.The excellent qualities is not the primary reason why everyone should see "The Help" , although it boasts a sharp and intelligent screenplay (Tate Taylor) based on a famous New York Times Best Seller book of the same name (Kathryn Stockett), a masterful directing (Tate Taylor), a grand ensemble of talented actors (see below), and a memorable soundtrack.What truly makes "The Help" a must-see movie is that it powerfully shows on the big screen something that most of us might have learned, heard, or read about, but have never experienced or seen with our own eyes. This is a case where a picture is worth a thousand words. It might be impossible for us to travel back in time and immerse ourselves into the ugly reality of the sophisticated humiliation and quiet suffering flourishing in Jackson, Mississippi in 1960s. "The Help" with its emotionally charged, vivid images and a compelling, thought provoking story manages to capture the essence of the historical and social setting. "The Help" is also a good lesson of humility for all of us. Nowadays it is easy to be disgusted and terrified by the atrocities that some well- educated, church going wealthy white folks were committing about 50 years ago in Jackson, Mississippi. It does not cost us anything to condemn what everyone now agrees was terribly wrong. We do not need to be brave, courageous, or insightful to do that.We might even consider ourselves a better, wiser, morally superior generation with a stronger self-awareness. A hard to swallow truth of the matter is that 50 years from now the future generation will be judging us. Good chances are that they will be just as disgusted and terrified with some of our behaviors, with our positions on certain social and moral issues, as we currently are terrified and disgusted with the behaviors of our ancestors.Despite what some critics say "The Help" is not a caricature or a simplistic social pamphlet with an obvious agenda. It maybe does not have a depth of a Dostoevsky's novel, but it is definitely offers a comprehensive, multidimensional view on a very complex problem. It successfully blends several well-developed story lines, each adding a unique value to the movie. Moreover, below the surface "The Help" has some strong thought-provoking undercurrents valuable for independent thinkers. One of these well-hidden but very powerful messages is a striking conflict between a pillar of the society Hilly Holbrook, representing the empty hypocrisy of the human religious traditions, and a quiet, ordinary African-America maid Aibileen Clark, representing the power of a genuine faith in God. On the outside, "Miss Hilly" (Bryce Dallas Howard, in a vivid and memorable performance) is one of the most respectable members of the society, a loving wife and a caring mother, a good Christian who is all about charity balls, helping poor African children, and giving proper respect and recognition to the "Help". On the inside, however, Hilly Holbrook is a hypocrite and a Chief Pharisee of the town. It does not take a long time for her ignorance and patronizing attitude to turn into a web of lies, cruelty, and deception. You could call her a monster, but please be aware that the same monster might be quietly (or not so quietly) growling inside of you. That is why Hilly Holbrook's moral failure becomes a stern warning of critical importance for all of us. Especially, if we do not want to hear the words that stunned her at the end of the movie: "You are a godless woman, Miss Hilly". And all the charity balls did not matter anymore. On the outside, Aibileen's life is terrible. She is alone. The worst imaginable personal tragedy has been haunting her from the past for many years. Her hard and valuable work that she pours her heart into is not noticed, appreciated, or even fairly compensated. She has to endure all sorts of abuse and humiliation both on the job and outside work. On the inside, however, something truly beautiful is happening with Aibileen (Viola Davis, in a strong, nuanced performance). Something allows her to show extraordinary love, courage, and faithfulness, to overcome both the personal tragedy and the hard life circumstances. Through many thoughtful, authentic details the movie makes is very clear to us who supports and empowers Aibileen all the way - the same God that Hilly Holbrook has somehow lost in the middle of her perfect high charitable life.knowyourmovie.blogspot.com</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>The black maids of Jackson, Mississippi, the "help."</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I realize this is a work of fiction, based on a book, but having lived through most of this, growing up in the south in the 1950s and 1960s, the story seems to be very plausible. The 1950s and 1960s that I knew, and depicted in this movie, were part of the "transition" years between slavery, which was abolished, and true freedom. Well-off white families had black maids, often referred to as "the help", paid relatively little and, at least depicted here, ordered around and expected to be slaves to their employer, even though they were "free" to leave. But they needed work, times were hard, and they often raised white children while other family members raised their own children. That is the situation in this movie, depicted in Jackson, Mississippi. Was Missisippi in the 1950s and 1960s the most racially bigoted state? I don't know, but a former co-worker who happens to be black attended Ole Miss back then and he said it was a very difficult environment.Emma Stone is aspiring journalist Skeeter Phelan, herself raised by a black maid (Cicely Tyson) who had almost become part of the family. Now Skeeter was back home after graduating from Ole Miss and began to clearly see the situation, and how bigoted her former friends were and how poorly maids were treated. She wanted to do something significant, to give voice to their plights, and sought to write the book, The Help, based on interviews she would do with maids. Perhaps the key maid character is Viola Davis as Aibileen Clark. The other is Octavia Spencer as Minny Jackson.Skeeter's friend and now housewife is Bryce Dallas Howard as Hilly Holbrook. Hilly becomes the main activist for keeping maids separate and "in their place" which includes a petition to require all domestic employers to have a separate bathroom for their black maids. As the story moves along we witness Skeeter and Hilly becoming former friends as their personal agendas drive them in different directions.The movie is well-written and well-acted. It tells an important story of that time. While it is a very serious, and often sad, subject the story is also sprinkled liberally with humor. Especially the "special" chocolate pie that Minny bakes for Hilly after Hilly had fired her.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>Howard Schumann</username>
		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>A realistic and often beautiful picture of the decaying South</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Based on the 2009 novel by Kathryn Stockett, Tate Taylor's The Help depicts the patience, loyalty, and courage of black maids who cooked, cleaned, and raised the children of white women for many decades and who were often subject to abuse and humiliation by their employers because of their race. Emma Stone's performance as a young writer who records the stories of several maids in a book she is writing plays a prominent, if not decisive, role in the film, yet it is mostly carried by the brilliant work of Viola Davis and Octavia Spencer whose performances as Aibileen and Minny are at the core of The Help's ability to stir deep human emotion.Filmed in Greenwood, Mississippi but set in Jackson, Cinematographer Stephen Goldblatt, aided by Production Designer Mark Ricker, creates a realistic and often beautiful picture of the decaying South with its dirt roads and once stately mansions now falling into disrepair. As the film opens, Aibileen Clark (Viola Davis) tells us that she is a maid like her mother before her and has raised seventeen white children as their surrogate mother. The film is Aibileen's story as well as that of her friend Minny (Octavia Spencer) as told to young Skeeter Phelan (Emma Stone), a college graduate who comes back to Mississippi with the intention of becoming a writer and quickly realizes that she no longer fits in with her former friends, the perky bridge-club types whose racism lies just below the surface.Taking a job at the Jackson Journal as the writer of a cleaning-advice column, a subject she knows little about, Skeeter turns to Aibileen for help. When Skeeter discovers that her mother (Allison Janney) has fired Constantine (Cicely Tyson), the maid who played such a loving role in her life for 29 years, she decides to write a book consisting of interviews with the maids of Jackson, hoping that they will tell their story for the first time in public. Bravely facing the danger of being seen in close contact with a white woman, Aibileen agrees to talk to Skeeter, writing down what she wants to say rather than responding to questions.Although Skeeter's publisher wants more maids to contribute, the risk of putting their lives on the line is an obstacle the others are not yet ready to confront. Soon, however, after a falling out with her employer, Minny agrees to join the project followed later by other maids, fed up with the way they are being treated. The project becomes a cathartic experience for the women, able to say out loud what they have been suppressing for a long time. While the portrayal of some of the white women in the film borders on caricature, for example the villainous Hilly (Bryce Dallas Howard), some of the more interesting characters are fully developed human beings.Prominent among this group are Celia (Jessica Chastain), a rich ditzy Marilyn Monroe type who hires Minny and is taught how to cook and clean house, Skeeter's mother Charlotte (Allison Janney) who seems to be one of the few who are able to learn from their mistakes, and Hilly's mother Missus Walters (Sissy Spacek), who is able to enjoy a good laugh at her daughter's expense when Hilly becomes the recipient of an extremely gross incident which, though it has a point to make (unlike most Hollywood gross-out comedies), goes on much longer than necessary.While The Help is not a documentary and does not purport to present an all-inclusive picture of the racially-motivated beatings, lynching, and routine killings in Mississippi in the early 20th century and into the 50s and 60s, it still accomplishes what it sets out to do ? to educate and entertain a generation largely unaware of the conditions of African-American women in the South before the movement for equality gained a foothold. While The Help may be sentimental (an unforgivable crime in some quarters these days), when Aibileen looks a little girl in the eyes and tells her: "You is kind. You is smart. You is important," every tear that may be shed has been dutifully earned. If that is called "pandering" to white folks, so be it.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Pulled short on any real content or poignancy</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Mmm. Oscars buzz is in the air like the smell of fresh baked pecan pie! First slice is The Help, a sort of alternative history of Jackson, Mississippi in the turbulent civil rights era.The Help has three main heroes: plucky Skeeter Phelan, serious Aibileen Clark, and feisty Minny Jackson. Skeeter returns to Jackson with the goal of being a writer. The story that interests her the most is that of The Help (ding!). She attempts to get Aibileen to divulge tales of running the households of white women, but Aibileen fears for her safety and refuses. However, circumstances surrounding the mistreatment of her friends leads Aibileen to begin spilling the beans. Other maids soon join in and BAM - best seller!At first glance this film is everything it attempts to be: a story touching on the racism of the day, a heartwarming plot driven by strong characters, and a bit of good fun in the process. However, I felt that the characters played as mere stereotypes, and the true feeling of the era was softened either by nostalgia on the part of the author, or intentionally as a means of selling a feel-good story.Each character's role, while well defined, never allows for any real change. Skeeter didn't ever have to build up courage to write The Help; she simply took some screen time convincing everybody else to get on board. It didn't change her already super-modern, open-minded viewpoint, and it didn't make her a champion of civil rights. She sold her story, reaped the benefits (with surprisingly few downsides), and moved on. Aibileen stayed the strong central figure behind the idea of getting African American maid's stories heard, but didn't stray from that serious face she wore so well. There were a few moments of laughter, lightness, but she took little convincing to put herself in harm's way and in the end, payed a very mild price for her revolt against the white system that controlled her life. Minny Jackson ? comic relief. Hilly Holbrook ? the racist villain. Celia Foote ? silly woman too simple to understand racism. Obviously a drama such as this will have some stereotypes, but it went all the way to the top.Hilly Holbrook seems to be the only real racist in the picture. She's young, angry, and determined to keep those sub-human colored people out of her bathroom, but not from handling every other part of her life. The movie portrays every other young elite woman as just following in step, not really concerned with race, but more with being socially accepted. Add to that every male and person over the age of 40 seems perfectly well balanced in their views on race. I'm not asking the movie to be a venom-filled, dark affair, but I think it paints Jackson in an untruthful light. I'm from the South, so is Queer. I know racists and they don't delicately prance around the issue today, and I'll put good money up that they didn't back in the '60s. Every bit on Kathryn Stockett talks about her upbringing in Jackson and her relationship with the African-American who raised her through the 1970s and in to the '80s. New York Times admits she pulls her punches on attacking the racism she witnessed, although they spin it as her being more "interested in the affection and intimacy buried beneath even the most seemingly impersonal household connections." I didn't get that from this 2 hour 20 minute film, but maybe the book covered the "affection and intimacy" in more detail.With all its problems ? minor plot lines wrapped up with a cheap line, a complete lack of any character growth ? it wasn't a bad film. It was heavy-handed (Oh look, a copy of To Kill a Mockingbird!), but it had some charm, entertainment value, and the southern accents didn't irk me nearly as much as I had expected. I do feel that my criticism is warranted. If you're going to tackle an issue like racism in the heart of the most racist parts of the country, you've got to commit. The Help simply lacked courage.Maybe I wanted the civil rights version of Inglorious Basterds, but this fictional history pulled short on any real content or poignancy.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>Alex_Pete_Georgie_Dim</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>Surprisingly Good For What It Is.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I am far from this films target audience. Most films that center around racial prejudice such as Crash, The Blind Side, or Remember The Titans tend to simplify the issue and tend to have the feeling of a T.V. movie or a class lesson video, so I don't usually hold them that high in regard. Which is why I was shocked at how much I ended up liking this film.The films plot centers around an aspiring journalist (played by Emma Stone) trying to write a book of stories about the lives of black maids working for rich, white families in Jackson, Mississippi during the civil rights movement. As such, it has the common themes of family, prejudice, acceptance, and understanding seen in these sort of films. The thing that sets it apart is the story and characters are generally well written. Everyone has well defined characters, even if some of them are a bit cliché, and they all act and react the way there personalities say they would. You can actually see these people existing in the real world, which allows you to connect with them much easier. As much of a trope as the stay-at- home, airhead 50's housewife is, I find it pretty entertaining, especially when such characters are fleshed out and interact with each other.On top of that, the film understands when to let the audience laugh. Even though it is mainly a drama, the humor in feels very genuine and constant, even if it can be a bit sit- com like at times. This is what separates it from films like Crash as it doesn't let the message drag the movie down and make it depressing. Also unlike Crash, The Help doesn't talk down to the audience. It gets it point across just through the flow of the story, without having to resort to dropping a giant anvil marked "racism is bad" on your head. It shows that there is more to racism than just hate. It can also come from societal pressure or be taught from birth. The movie respects your intelligence, and that's a rare thing to find in films these days.The film gives you drama and a few laughs and treats its subject matter with care. The characters are well written and well acted. To top it all off, you got a soundtrack that has Johnny Cash, Bo Diddley, and even a little Bob Dylan. What's not to like. Consider this a strong recommendation.</fullReview>

		<username>Robert J. Maxwell (rmax304823@yahoo.com)</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>Humble Help -- So Hard To Find, These Days.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Jackson, Mississippi, in 1963 and the segregation is iron bound. A feisty young local journalist manqué is hired by the local newspaper to write a household advice column. This prompts in her an interest in the black maids who service the community of higher-class white housewives. A volcano erupts.You see, the white folk live in them big white houses and the white womens they have dare hairs up in piles on top of they head and they demands that the silber be polished just proper for when they entertaining. The black folk lives in them shotgun houses in they own districk and eats fried chicken and rabbit stews all day. They need them maid jobs powerful bad because the menfolk, they don't make no money toiling at the mill.I know. It sounds like an afternoon television special that should be shown in a civics class, but it's better than that. The first thing that impresses the viewer, after the initial exposition, is the presence of social borders that are more effective in keep people in and out than the Great Wall of China was at defeating Mongol raiders. The social world is divided into strict categories.It's a binary universe. You are either "in" or "out." You are black or you are white. You are a man or you are a woman. You are respectable or you are white trash. There isn't much in between. (Poor Truman Capote.) And within each of these socially constructed categories, there is an intense sense of schedule, of the proper time to do things in the proper way, the time for politesse, the time for slap downs. The heroine, the would-be writer, is an affable and outspoken redhead with frizzly hair, given to wearing Mexican shoes and not yet married, although she's in her twenties. Her mother, Allison Janney, nervously asks her, "Honey, it's past your time and you have no boy friends. Tell me, have you ever had any, um, unnatural feelings? Like feeling towards girls?" That's scheduling for you. And here's social class. A naive but pretty and friendly young blond, Jessica Chastain, moves into town, drinks too much, wears revealing gowns, speaks a little too freely, and can't cook anything but corn pone. When she shows up with a covered dish at a party by the local doyen from etiquette hell, nobody answers the door. They hide from her view behind the furniture and speak in whispers. As one of the well-groomed guests observes, she's "white trash." Well, let us not run out of space by going on too long about the keenly observed characters and plot, which is weakened only in the last quarter or so, when everyone winds up happy except the witch. The witch's performance is splendid, by the way, but then everyone involved gives an unusually tuned performance. The editor has caught Viola Davis, as the principle maid, doing some marvelous acting. Sadly, there is the hint of cloying stereotypes. Man, the director has gotten respectable Southern women and their cliques down pat, but he overdoes it with the nameless horde of carefully made-up butterflies mincing around, fluttering their delicate hands and turning every sentence into a melodic carol that ends in a question mark. And the African-Americans, while human and hardly saints, don't seem to have anything in the way of vices. They simply accept the insults and burn with unspoken resentment.Of course, the central theme is the way maids are treated as robots and the way the robots take their revenge. The black maids receive what sociologists call "non-person treatment." They're not really there, and the exchanges are either functional ("Get me an iced tea?") or perfunctory ("That's a mighty purdy dress, Mizz Scarlet.") Those social borders are noisome. For one thing, they have two sides. After hundreds of years of being bulked up, they now operate to effectively keep categories comfortably apart. African-Americans in particular have a solidarity that other minorities can barely understand. It's asking a great deal of them to sacrifice that hard-earned group loyalty in the name of an equality that many of them see as a façade. Would you want your daughter to marry one? Neither side is especially anxious for that to happen.I'm not even certain that the tendency to feel more comfortable with others who share your appearance and culture is so unnatural. Every big city has its Chinatown and Little Italy. And when "Planet of the Apes" was being filmed, the chimpanzees ate with the other chimpanzees, the gorillas ate with the gorillas, and the gibbons hung out with the gibbons. Maybe it's better not to think about it. It's certainly EASIER not to.</fullReview>

Skeeter(Emma Stone)a fresh Ol Miss graduate returns home to where her mother is eagerly awaiting with the major thing on her mind is to find Skeeter a husband. However, Skeeter is determined to become a writer and gets her start by taking the help/advice column for the local paper. Skeeter wants something more substantial and begins the greatest experience of her life. With help from her best friend's maid, Aibileen(Viola Davis), she decides on writing about the true unhappiness of the many black women who have spent their lives taking care of prominent white southern families. This undertaking begins slow, but Aibileen convinces more of her friends to come forward. In secret Skeeter and the maids collaborate on putting their stories to paper. In spite of Skeeter's white friends, she has definitely forged a sisterhood with the willing maids that eagerly await the finished product...Skeeter's novel. The high class women of Jackson can't put the book down, unassured that it is actually about them through the eyes of their own maids. Whispers can make a change. THE HELP makes you want to read the novel if you didn't read it already. A very strong cast in a very entertaining film. It's one of those rare films that sneaks up on you and crawls deep into your heart. It poignantly addresses important social issues without casting judgment and allows its vibrant characters to unashamedly display their weaknesses. Tate Taylor's direction is heartfelt and his well crafted screenplay gloriously magnifies Kathryn Stockett's critically acclaimed No. 1 New York Times best-selling debut novel. The cast shines in their respective roles, particularly Viola Davis as Aibileen, the heart and soul of the film, and Octavia Spencer as out-spoken Minny Jackson. 		<username>yvonne-158-15918 (yvonne@thebrooklynmouth.com)</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Shallow Treatment of a Complex Subject with Strong Performances</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I haven't read the best selling book the movie The Help is based on. Nor have I paid much attention to the controversy about a White central character telling the story of black maids in Jackson, Mississippi. I can only judge the movie by what appears on screen. You get the sense the audience is being manipulated. This is where you're supposed to laugh; this is where you should cry. It feels like a paint by numbers movie lesson about the ills of racism that comes across as a bit shallow and sunny despite the subject matter. There is a moment in the film where you see a newspaper headline about the Freedom Riders who were pioneers in the civil rights movement. If you've seen the powerful and unforgettable PBS documentary about these real life heroes, The Help will come across as a flimsy and pale attempt at depicting a complex and disturbing issue that still reverberates today. What the movie does have going for it are the strong performances of the mainly female cast. Emma Stone is earnest and full of luminous spunk as Skeeter Phelan the author of these women's tales. Viola Davis is understated and deeply touching as Aibileen, the first maid to open up to Skeeter about what it's like to serve in a White household. Octavia Spencer in a standout role as Minny Jackson is funny and fierce as the maid abused by both her husband and employer Hilly Holbrook. Bryce Dallas Howard as Hilly conveys the essence of a cold heart wrapped in femininity to perfection. Jessica Chastain takes her character, Celia Foote the girl from the wrong side of the tracks and imbues her desire to be accepted with both sweetness and pain. Cicely Tyson is unforgettably moving in her flashback scenes as Constantine the Phelan family's lifelong maid and Skeeter's surrogate mother.The Help is designed to be a crowd pleaser that leaves the audience saying can you believe how these people used to behave in the South and that's the problem because segregation and discrimination were not only problems confined to the South. And, although we've come a long way since 1963, I couldn't fail to note that many in the audience where I watched the film have Black nannies raising their children. The movie is just a little too satisfied with itself and I don't think we're ready to be patting ourselves on the back just yet.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Simple, ordinary and chilling</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Bryce Dallas Howard chews the scenery, Violet Davis and Octavia Spencer seem to have sprung, passionate and inexorable as trees-of-life. Jessica Chastain stirs my empathy and sympathy as an erst-while poor white young lady who desperately needs the help and then some of an African-American maid. The breezy, affluent white men of the deep South cruise along seemingly oblivious of racial trouble just between the veneer of Southern hospitality. It amazes me how the human soul can become inured to almost anything. I can remember traveling in the old streetcars in downtown Detroit, never making eye contact with any persons of color - as if that was just the most natural attitude or perspective one could have, minister's preaching that that stance represented God's will. Movies like "Long Walk Home" and "Something the Lord Had Made" touched me more poignantly, when, for example, in the latter, Black people nonchalantly step off the sidewalk to allow white folks to traipse on by. In "Long Walk Home" we feel the pain of a Black maid who walked in heels for 15 miles. Bryce Dallas Howard is the repository human lightning rod in this film in terms of gathering in the viewer's sense of outrage, injustice and disgust. Like any good tragedy, it is the villain that makes the story work, and Howard could very well be nominated for a best-supporting Oscar for her inimical artistry in this film. I guess the most disturbing facet of this movie lurks behind the curtain: the fact that actresses as gifted as Davis and Spencer may not be able to find enough roles that Hollywood is willing to market, in this economy, in this society. It is sad that when art clashes with profit, the latter most always takes the field. This film is inhabited by consummate artists who can do more with an arched eyebrow, a look in the eye or merely by their very stance - than actors of lesser prowess can manage with scintillating scripts.</fullReview>
