		<shortDescription>2011's best drama</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>At the outset, I have to say that I have not read the novel by Kathyrn Stockett. Written for the screen and directed by her good friend Tate Taylor, The Help is one of the must see movies of the year.Strung together on a modest budget of 35 million, it has become one of the sleeper hits of the year with a domestic gross of over 160 million and still counting. Set in the background of 1960's civil rights struggle in America led by Martin Luther King, The Help focuses on the plight of African American housekeeper community and struggles against their racist white employers in suburban conservative Jackson,Mississippi. With an all star outstanding cast, it tells the story of a single independent white woman (Emma Stone) and her dogged pursuit to bring to light the plight of black cleaning maid community. When two of the help of the conservative households in Jackson come forward to tell their story, the struggles threads a thin line between fight for survival and a better future and fear of death from the dreaded and bigoted Ku Klux Klan. Portrayed brilliantly by Viola Davis and Octavia Spencer, the desperation and fear of the marginalised and oppressed Black community comes to fore with outstanding performances which is a sure bet for supporting actress nominations for both the actresses. The ensemble cast includes Bryce Dallas Howard, Jessica Chastain, Chris Lowell,Alison Janney and veteran actresses Sissy Spacek and Cicely Tyson .The performance of Cicely Tyson is one of the most the emotionally poignant scenes of the movie and though she has a screen time of just under two and half minutes, her performance is bound to make you reach for the tissues.The Help is a movie which will highlight how bad it was in the 1960's and how far we have come albeit with a long way to go to still achieve equality for all marginalised communities.The movie will make u get angry, frustrated, it will make u cry, and feel for the desperation of the maids and still manages to have its feel good moments.It is by far the best drama yet this year.</fullReview>

		<username>weesiedret08</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>My memories of this time in our history</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I think the strength and measure of this movie is not in how closely it followed the book, or whether the characters were one-dimensional or fully developed, but rather in what memories it evoked in the viewer. I was a college freshman in 1963, so I well remember and was reminded of the unfairness of that time. I particularly remember a heated discussion I got into with a Sunday School teacher. I don't remember how it began, but it devolved into her saying to me, among other things, "Well, you wouldn't a colored person to spend the night in your home! And surely you wouldn't want to eat a meal in their house!" I thought this was just plain stupid, and said so. In fact, the maid who worked for us had spent several nights in our home when my mother was at the hospital with my grandmother. And she cooked my meals at our house, and I truly couldn't see the difference in that and eating at her house. By the time I met up with Mother for church, word had already reached her of my disrespect to the SS teacher. She just looked at me and said, "MUST you do things like that?" But she never told me not to. I think she was torn between agreeing with me and not wanting me to be disrespectful of my elders.Incidentally, I was informed that one referred to "coloreds" as "women," not "ladies." I asked why, and was informed that that was just the way it was. Once again, I replied with, "Stupid!" I also remembered the water fountains in the local Montgomery Ward. The shiny, new, water cooler had a sign above it that said, "Whites," while the old, stained, chipped water fountain (not cooler!) had a sign that said, "Coloreds." How reprehensible! I did not know from those days that some whites did not want or allow their maids to use the toilets in their homes. So I actually learned something from the movie.When I saw this movie today, sitting on the row behind me were 3 black ladies who were about the age to have been maids during the '60s. I felt such shame, wondering what painful experiences they must have been remembering.I would have liked to have seen more comments in these reviews about people's personal reactions and memories, and less synopsizing of the story.This is one movie I will look forward to purchasing when it becomes available on DVD.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Give it a chance &amp; make up your own mind</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Has some of the best acting ever and can be appreciated for that fact alone. There's a lot of controversy over using black life in the Old South as a backdrop for a young white woman's self-awakening. It's certainly guilty as charged on that count, but it's so high-quality it can still be enjoyed as a movie and as entertainment. It's not posing as a documentary, it's a Hollywood tear-jerking, inspirational, feel-good film with some fantastic characters and performances all the way around.Yes, I'm aware the book never happened, and that there are some excellent reasons why not. A real-life Skeeter would've been quickly discovered and discouraged - to put it mildly. The police and the Klan (often the same men) would've also firebombed some of the maids' houses and probably the church, too, just for good measure. At least one of the maids would've suffered terribly before being murdered or framed and imprisoned. So it's also a Hollywood fantasy, albeit a terrifically realized one. But guess what? Name your favorite movies and see how well they live up to reality. How many mob bosses were anything like Don Vito or Michael Corleone? Could Mexican farmers round up seven great gunfighters to risk their lives for them for $20 apiece? Or make it seven samurai doing the same thing for a bowl of rice a day. How closely did the Malcolm X movie actually follow his autobiography? See what we're getting into here? Like my father once said: "You pay your money and sit down and then you decide for yourself how much of it you want to believe." The point here is to just relax and enjoy some epic acting - not hambone emoting, but truly nuanced portrayals and skillfully designed and actualized characterizations. Give "The Help" a chance and make up your own mind about whether you like it or not.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>A Movie That Helps To Expose The Racial Divide That Yet Remains</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The unintentional and revealing shock of this movie isn't how the trailers and previews misled the audience as to the nature of this movie, but that the marketing arm and distributor felt that it was necessary (perhaps rightly so) to depict the movie more as primarily a comedy rather than a drama in the first place. Fifty years after this period piece takes place, the irony is that the emotional drama and power of this movie arises for its "contemporary" audience from the likelihood that we can be shocked by this movie at all and its ability to push the audience's racial button after all this time. Like the hidden embarrassment of sexual products on commercial television, this movie seems to have dared to expose the dirty laundry in a balanced drama, lightened with elements of humor.Unlike The Stepford Wives (2004) that used comedy as its primary platform to expose the dichotomy of the 1950s with todays societal values, The Help in the same way Uncle Tom's Cabin published in 1852 (there were not movies back then) brings to the mainstream audience a serious but balanced and mainstream acceptable version of the racial divide of the 1950s. The Help is a break through movie for our times that oddly enough may or may not hold up in 50 or 100 years unless it becomes memorable for its revelation at least in mainstream movies, the racial blindness of its history America still retained so many years later at the Civil Rights era. Or maybe like Schindler's List (1993), the shocking but revealing World War II drama, The Help may be able to retain its historical value aside from its present day exposure of today's America's subconscious.The Help is a deeply revealing, emotional compelling inward look at America and allows mainstream America to become sensitively aware of the thin veneer that remains covering over the racial prejudice that still pervades America today, even as the gains made over the past 30,40 years have been wiped away, especially with the Great Recession and the political atmosphere that has become a backdrop of everyday existence for African Americans and other minorities in this Country.</fullReview>

If the filmmakers wanted to be an historically accurate think-piece, they really should not have made the trailers make the movie seem like a comedy. If they wanted it to be a comedy, they shouldn't have included some of the more vicious racial elements. It ended up being a movie that made you laugh at parts and really made you squirm in your seat at other parts but left you feeling like you were robbed of something that could have been really great. All that being said, however, it was a pretty good main-stream movie touching on the subject of household help and the racial tensions involved in the nascent Civil Rights era of the 50s and 60s that I haven't seen before. It was interesting to see how these women so bonded with their employer's children and how the children saw their maids as their second (or even "real") mothers.Basically, it was a good movie but don't expect to learn much from it.</fullReview>

		<username>jessiharman</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>LOVE This must see movie</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>If this film were total fiction bearing no relation to reality, it would still be worth seeing for the fine acting and production values--even if some of the young white women approached "Southern Gothic." But it wasn't fiction--at least, the depiction of Southern society wasn't. As I watched I kept drifting back to small-town South Carolina in the 1950s, where I grew up. It was moving and disturbing to be reminded how black people were treated then--loved and yet "kept down in their place." Our neighborhood was all middle-class and every family had a maid. There were plenty of boys my age, we visited in each other's homes, and called every maid by her first name. One even started a baseball team for the little white boys, for which her reward was a visit by the Klan.Our maid helped my mother cook and clean. One of my parents picked her up and took her home every day--and she rode in the back seat. She ate her lunch in our kitchen--without being allowed to use our utensils. I remember her eating with her fingers. I do not remember ever seeing her use our bathrooms. I thought about that during the movie and truly cannot recall what she did, an embarrassing gap in memory.I do remember when my father was out of work and our maid had to be cut back to three days a week. I actually cried; she was a member of our family. When talk about civil rights began in the late 1950s, my mother became annoyed at our maid for getting "uppity." And so it went. We moved to central Florida in 1961, where there were no maids.Travel back in time with this film. It's quite real, and I highly recommend it.</fullReview>

		<date>2016-08-01</date>
		<username>ConsultingHobbitExtractors</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>Better than you would expect.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Maybe you believe that there is no need for yet another movie about black segregation and the civil rights movement and think that it has no pertinence to modern society's form of racism, but you have to admit that this was a decently done movie. At the very least, it avoids many of the pitfalls that other racial segregation movies fall into. It demonizes a pretty small amount of white characters, and aptly addresses how peer pressure was more important than hatred in continued segregation. Also, it only focuses partially on race. The selling point of the movie is the racial aspect, but it's more about being a maid and getting the story told. We don't hate Hilly Holbrook because she's racist, we hate her because she is unappreciative of the work that Minny does for her.All of the characters are easy to follow, which is remarkable considering how many important characters there are. Even when they were talked about by other characters, I had no trouble figuring out who the characters were referring to. You won't need to know everyone's name, but you likely will know 80% of the names. Even the absence of Constantine, who only makes one brief and profound appearance, has an important role to the plot that is exposed well enough to understand easily. We get unique emotional access to every character, almost none of which is accomplished by means of racism. One character point that was really good was Abilene's attachment to the children she raised. It showed how much pride she took in her work despite her disgust at Mrs. Leefolt, and also let us know that she didn't just remain a maid out of necessity.The story is extremely character driven, which is mostly possible because it is based on fiction and not on any historical events. A historical time period, yes, but that's different than being tied down to an event that has to be shown and slows the plot down. I got the sense that the author is not that fond of southern culture in general, especially since Skeeter is the protagonist and is trying to break away from that culture. One viable quip that can be made is that the black maids are made to be too perfect. This could be said for Abilene, but Minny brings plenty of humanity to balance this out. Not faults exactly, but we definitely don't get the sense that Minny is perfect in any way. The same goes for Skeeter, who isn't black but is on the side of the maids. I suppose you could also say that it is partial against men, since all three men that have any kind of importance are made to seem kind of stupid. But then again, guys are stupid, and the women had plenty of faults themselves.Those issues aside, it's a very engaging story that keeps you interested and even entertained at several points. The pacing is great and there's a good balance of how much each character is shown. It's a bit long, especially for the genre and feels longer because of how the story continues on for a while after the book is published. The acting is great from almost every part. I thought Emma Stone and Allison Janney deserved Oscar nominations just as much as Jessica Chastain.Don't choose this if you're looking for something about the civil rights movement. If you hate movies like that, I would suggest that you give this one twenty minutes. There's minimal white guilt-tripping and racism is only a part of the plot, not the whole thing. It's a good family movie despite the PG-13 rating. Also, it's good to watch if you want to go into any form of journalism or communications field. Overall Rating: 8.3/10.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>'The Help' Packs In Some Serious Girl Power And Is One Of The Best Films Of Its Year</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Crafted with care, told with composure &amp; powered by strong feminine energy, The Help is a wicked combination of comedy &amp; drama that manages to touch on serious issues without ever discarding its funny side. Entertaining &amp; enlightening in equal doses, this period drama is a roller-coaster ride of emotions that delivers a powerful jolt while also boasting moments of genuine laughter.Set during the 1960s, the story of The Help follows a young woman who aspires to be a successful writer and, after witnessing the poor treatment of African-American maids from their employers on a daily basis, decides to reveal their side of the story by writing a book detailing their experiences. Assisting to bring her noble work to life are two maids who work for her white socialite friends.Written &amp; directed by Tate Taylor, the film packs a few dense moments in between but maintains a lighthearted tone for the majority of its runtime. Taylor could have gone for a serious, hard-hitting drama about the racial injustice &amp; hardships these housemaids go through on a daily basis but the witty dimension gives this tale an accessible layer that gets the job done without trying too hard.Production design team does a fab job at recreating the required 1960s setting, Cinematography is expertly carried out, for the images brim with warm palette &amp; bright lighting while fluid movements of the camera keep the drama sharp &amp; resonant. Its 146 minutes runtime is never felt, thanks to its steady pacing &amp; first-rate editing. And Thomas Newman's score is able to evoke the apt emotions with its serene tracks.Coming to the performances, The Help features a powerful ensemble in Emma Stone, Viola Davis, Octavia Spencer, Bryce Dallas Howard, Jessica Chastain, Allison Janney &amp; Sissy Spacek, and all of them get their moment to shine. Davis delivers the most powerful performance of them all, and is closely followed by Spencer. Stone is absolutely adorable, Dallas Howard does well with what she is given, while Chastain pretty much steals the show &amp; is my absolute favourite here.On an overall scale, The Help is a skilfully directed, deftly written, brightly photographed, smartly edited, calmly composed &amp; ingeniously performed cinema that could've done a tad more with its provocative themes but in the end, it still succeeds as a rich example of its genre that's enjoyable, amusing &amp; illuminating. Sophisticated in all filmmaking departments, strengthened by a casting powerhouse &amp; keeping a firm grip on the viewers' senses, The Help is one of the best films of its year that's worth your time &amp; money.</fullReview>

		<username>klwoyurk</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>*Contains Spoilers!** Summary and Visual Analysis on The Help</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>This movie based on a novel originally written by Kathryn Stockett, is showcased in three southern women residing in Jackson, Mississippi in the early 1960's. The movie showcases a spectrum of individual personalities in the lives of Aibileen, Minny and Skeeter (known as Eugenia Phelan.) The movie was primarily focused on the struggles these women faced in a very racially biased south and the adversities women faced on the job market in the 1960's. Stockett created beautiful waves in how she made individuals aware of the ugliness that can happen in life, but the beauty that occurs within that ugliness when times are unforgiving. In the case I'm thinking about, Skeeter, played by Emma Stone, was an aspiring journalist living in Jackson on the forefront of race issues in the south. Skeeter, while searching for her dream job, was also searching for Constantine, her family's loyal maid that was suddenly "let go." In the search for Constantine, Skeeter realized that the maids whom supply services to her friends (essentially labeled "the help") are treated extremely differently than white employees. Later, Skeeter decides to create a column in a local newspaper to display this "mistreatment" and unfairness being done by the families of these maids in the south. Skeeter gets the "okay" to do this through having tips also written within the text in order to gain authority from her primarily white readers.In the column, Skeeter decides to use her comrades to tell their stories in order to obtain excessive attention with hopes to change the trend of blacks being extremely mistreated and unfairly rewarded for their services. Aibileen, a maid and partial nanny, agrees to share her misfortunes and tips for being a maid in the column displayed in their local newspaper. After endless efforts to get more maids and "help" on board with the project, Aibileen's friend, Minny also agrees to share her adversities. Since the column in the local paper was so scrutinized by the individuals who employ the maids and nannies, Skeeter essentially put her works and the words of the help into a book, published it, and entitled it, The Help. (Shmoop.com, 2015)The novel and movie truly opened eyes in a different light. Stockett focused on the women that suffered through the race imbalances in the south. In doing this, a white woman and two black women worked together toward a common goal: to forever cease the harsh realities of being a poorly-paid and treated maid in the deep south during the '60's and '70's. Viewers are able to witness comedy and heart-felt emotions all while receiving a not-so-common history lesson. Not only were these women facing excruciatingly harsh realities in regards to race, but on the job front as well. Skeeter, a young white female was unable to find work for a long period of time. Individuals in the south were not comfortable with allowing someone to essentially "choose the wrong side" and make that information public. Stockett really grand slammed this novel turned into a movie because of the bases she covered. One scene I remember vividly in the movie occurred when Minny, a maid of individuals who treated her below them, though really hilarious, acted in a way that only she could justify. In this scene, Minny baked a pie that she personally delivered to Hilly Holbrook, her house employer. At the end of the movie, it is apparent and learned that inside the pie that Minny baked for the villainous Holbrook, Minny placed her own feces in it. It's little (disgusting) acts displayed like that which really bring humor into a very serious issue. Additionally, Minny gets abused by her husband each night, another adversity she faces in addition to her job. (Shmoop.com, 2015) After I read the first couple chapters of this book, I knew I wanted to see the movie. It is fascinating to me how little individuals know about things that are sublets of the bigger pictures. Sure, we all learned about the endless imbalances of racial issues in the south and across America, especially in the 1950's through the 1970's. But who ever actually thought of the jobs within that conflict that showcased women specifically? It's very moving to me that Stockett focused a novel turned movie on something so miniscule in regards to the "bigger picture" of race, but so damaging to so many women's lives. I admire that she wrote about three different women of more than one race working together to achieve and tackle a goal much bigger than themselves. The Help showcases how America was very close-minded and stubborn to the fact that women of more than one race were gasping for some kind of hold of society and were never rewarded it. Through Skeeter's work and the stories of Minny and Aibileen, The Help was a success and showcased more than enough history for me to open my eyes.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>Revenance</username>
		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>Spectacular look at racism in the '60s!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The Help is directed &amp; written by Tate Taylor and features an A-list ensemble led by Emma Stone, Viola Davis, and Octavia Spencer. The film is set in Jackson, Mississippi during the early '60s of the U.S., back when the country was still segregated, and follows avid college graduate Eugenia "Skeeter" Phelan who earns a job as a columnist for a local newspaper to fulfill her aspirations of becoming a writer. However, as she comes to know the black household maids belonging to her closest friends, including friendly Aibileen (Viola Davis) &amp; sassy Minny (Octavia Spencer), as well as the lives they have to deal with every day, she decides to do something incredibly important yet out of law: publish a book consisting of stories from the largest number of negro maids she can. Along the way, unlikely friendships are made, some bonds are broken, and the truth will inspire.The poppy poster for this film screams "chick flick!", but do not let it mislead you like it did for me. This is a film I implore you to watch. What we have here is an very underrated, truthful, smart, emotional showcase that is handled with such vivid beauty by everyone involved. I can safely say this is one of the most well-executed &amp; well-written films I've come across in some years. Tate Taylor's last film was this largely ignored indie, but this one will truly bring him to light. Why? Because he brings the troubled and evocative experiences of all parties to light magnificently.They can come from something as anger-inducing as a racist bridge club's bill preposition for a separate bathroom in every white home to something as raw as a white 4-year-old thinking of her maid as her mother. In fact, that strong but risky relationship is such a solid and emotional drive for the story as well as a flesher. When Skeeter is devastated to learn that her lifelong maid Constantine was fired by her prissy mother because of old age, it immediately follows with a flashback that establishes the special connection that they shared. When the maid's fate is presented later, it is arguably one of the most emotionally powerful "quiet" scenes I've seen. Even Aibileen finds it hard to think of leaving her master's little daughter, Mobley Mae. Aibileen's true character compared to Mae's mother is breathtakingly established with Mae's words to the maid, "You are my real mom."Many people are criticizing how the majority of characters here are stereotypical; yes, they're all different people common in a segregated society, but thanks to the creative and acting department, each one of these people, even the ones that start out hackneyed, are given layers of flesh and depth that makes each one of their stories a thoughtful yet entertaining journey. Both Viola Davis and Emma Stone are pushed to their respective powers, exhibiting quiet courage and fierce respect for one another. Bryce Dallas Howard, portraying a racist club leader, makes her Hilly Holbrook such a clever and hateful antagonist. The elderly black who plays Constantine shines in her short scenes. But Octavia Spencer and Jessica Chastain's work has got to be the most intriguing and interesting relationship in the film. Spencer lends a cautious wit and an unstoppable comedic spark to Minny, an already well-realized character, and it's amazing to watch her entire perspective &amp; approach towards whites change during her service for kind but social outcast Celia Foote (Chastain). A particular scene where Foote willingly sits at the same makeshift table with Minny establishes that there's soul to be found in both races, absolutely true. My only real problem with the film is that its sense of realism is slightly off. Certain situations that are overly racist seem too evil to be true. Tech-wise, Thomas Newman delivers a perfect score as always, having delved into my soul in 2013's Saving Mr. Banks. It takes a little more time than usual to be fully immersed into the American life of segregation, but once racism and all of those issues are realized on screen, then the film will have you on a hook. I was honestly adamant about seeing this movie, thinking it would be this sappy boring drag made for young ladies, but, man, there was not one dull moment of screen-time wasted here, everyone was perfect in the role, and it hits just the right mark on heartfelt impact. I can see why it got that Best Picture nod in 2011...it's one of the best of that year.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>Danusha_Goska Save Send Delete</username>
		<score>0.6</score>
		<shortDescription>Pretty Soap Opera with Magical Negroes</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I enjoyed "The Help" but for all the wrong reasons. I loved the high production values, the beautiful lighting, the shots of ripe agricultural fields in the Deep South. I especially loved the exquisite, tailor-fit, vintage, early 1960s dresses worn by the white actresses. I got a huge kick out of the over-the-top performances. Bryce Dallas Howard, though a living person, is every bit the Walt Disney cartoon villainess. You love hating her. You grimace when she cackles and you cackle as she reaches her inevitable, and utterly unbelievable, nightmare, revenge-dream-fantasy downfall. Howard should have won an academy award. Viola Davis, who radiates soul and depth and can make you cry just by walking into a room, is the deep, rich heart of this otherwise silly film. Octavia Spencer is adorable. She's as big-eyed and woebegone as a DW Griffith Gish sister. I have no idea why Emma Stone is the star. She can't act much and she isn't a drop dead beauty. Maybe she knows someone. "The Help" is about black-white relations in Mississippi in the early 1960s. Jim Crow Mississippi was a terrorist state. That sounds like an extremist statement; it is not. The Mississippi Sovereignty Commission planted spies in every nook and cranny of Mississippi life. It aided at least one assassin, Byron de la Beckwith, who murdered Medgar Evers. This deadly serious, tense, historic era is reduced, in "The Help," to a very pretty soap opera featuring magical Negroes ? a term used to describe black characters in mostly white films who exist only to provide a means for the main, white character to grow. You notice immediately in "The Help" that the white women are all slim, elegantly and flatteringly dressed, and pretty. The black women are plump, older, and plain. But, this is an uplifting film about Civil Rights, so all the black women are noble, giving, selfless, and saintly, while all, except one, of the whites are vicious, vacuous, selfish, and bad mothers. Also, all the whites are filthy rich. Was it really that way, you have to ask? No, it wasn't. Mississippi has one of the highest white poverty rates in the US. And some whites joined the Civil Rights struggle, performing the ultimate sacrifice, and giving their own lives. Still waiting for a feature film about Viola Liuzzo. Maybe it's not such a bad thing that simplistic and overtly exploitative movies like this get made. If they interest any viewers in the Civil Rights movement, that's not a bad thing. Maybe it will inspire some to see a truly worthy film series, "Eyes on the Prize."</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>The Help</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I saw the trailer for this film based on the acclaimed novel and I had mixed opinions and was unsure whether I'd want to see it, but then came the awards season, and I was definitely interested to see if the winner for Best Supporting Actress was deserved. Basically, set in Mississippi in the 1960s, during the time of the Civil Rights movement, middle-aged black maid Aibileen Clark (Oscar, BAFTA and Golden Globe nominated Viola Davis) recently lost her son, and she has raised many white children in her life for the families she works for. Another black maid has been fired numerous times because of her outspokenness; Minny Jackson (Oscar, BAFTA and Golden Globe winning Octavia Spencer) does however have fantastic cooking skills. After graduating from the University of Mississippi, young white woman Skeeter Phelan (Emma Stone) has returned home, and she is surprised her childhood black maid Constantine Jefferson (Cicely Tyson) has quit her job, she questions why she did not write before leaving. All her friends from university have got married and had children, but Skeeter is single and keen to start a career as a writer, she gets her first job for the local paper writing "homemaker hints" columns. Aibleen is the maid of Skeeter's friend Elizabeth Leefolt (Ahna O'Reilly), Skeeter notices that the maids are treated as nothing more than the "help"; she is uncomfortable with the attitudes towards them, especially from Hilly Holbrook (Bryce Dallas Howard) who is racist and sees black people as a disease. As one of the few in the country that sees black maids as equal citizens of society Skeeter decides to write a novel based on their lives and their time taking care of white children. Most maids are uninterested in being interviewed and talking about their live, they are worried they will lose their jobs, but Aibleen is open to share her experiences, after Hilly's harsh comments, and the children she looks after are becoming like their parents. Minny gets fired by Hilly after ignoring the rule to use a separate toilet, and she finds it impossible to find work with her reputation being poisoned, and her daughter ends up becoming a maid as well. Minny initially says no to answering questions for the writing of a book, but she does agree, and Aibleen helps find her job working with Celia Foote (Oscar, BAFTA and Golden Globe nominated Jessica Chastain) as a maid, Foote is married to socialite Johnny (Mike Vogel), and with a working class background and her husband being Hilly's ex is an outcast, but she treats black maids with respect. A draft of the book is finished by Skeeter, but Harper &amp; Row editor Elain Stein (Mary Steenburgen) in New York City says the book would only get interest with at least a dozen interviews from black maids, with the Civil Rights Movement she should do this soon. It is after the assassination of Medgar Evans and Hilly firing her latest black maid that many more do come forward to talk about themselves, they believe the book will allow them to have their voices heard during the time of all the tension between races. Minny also includes one last story to ensure no-one will reveal where the stories came from, she confesses that she baked a chocolate pie for Hilly, not long after being fired, accused of stealing, and, in front of Missus Walters (Sissy Spacek) as well, after eating two slices the maid reveals to Hilly that she baked her own excrement into the pie. Skeeter also adds her own story about Constantine and being brought up by her, and she finds out from her mother Charlotte (Allison Janney) that she was fired as a maid during a reception, but she felt guilty and sent Skeeter's brother to find her in Chicago living with her daughter Rachel, but Constantine was dead. The book is finished, and after being published the writer and the maids are pleased that it becomes an instant success, all the royalties are shared between the author and those that participated, and Skeeter is pleased to be offered a job in a New York City publishing company, but her boyfriend Stuart Whitworth (Chris Lowell) who does not believe in racial equality breaks up with her. Hilly is hatching a plan to get rid of Aibleen as Elizabeth's "help", getting her accused of stealing silverware, and she is successful in getting her fired, but she agrees to leave, even after giving her opinions of Hilly's vindictiveness and pleading her innocence, Elizabeth's daughter Mae Mobley is so upset, but Aibleen looks to go and find a new and better life for herself. Also starring The Last King of Scotland's David Oyelowo as Preacher Green, Anna Camp as Jolene French, Aunjanue Ellis as Yule Mae Davis and True Blood's Nelsan Ellis as Henry the Waiter. Spencer certainly deserved her Oscar for the great scenes she is in whether serious or funny, the most memorable of course being the pie sequence, Davis could have won as well for her emotional and established lead performance, Stone is a great choice to play the woman writing the novel to hopefully make some change, and many supporting cast members are great too, I'd say particularly Howard as the nasty snobbish ringleader with disrespectful and very racist views. It is a terrific film told from the perspectives of the black women who want to speak out for all black people who are mistreated in the world and the woman hoping to help them speak out for those suffering the torment, and it has all the emotions, good and bad, to make a well-paced and most worthwhile drama. It was nominated the Oscar for Best Motion Picture of the Year, and it was nominated the BAFTAs for Best Film and Best Adapted Screenplay, and it was nominated the Golden Globe for Best Motion Picture - Drama and Best Original Song for Mary J. Blige's "The Living Proof". Worth watching!</fullReview>

Without doubt it's well played/acted, but, the story is where it goes sour in my opinion. What I said in the summary, yawn, it simply does not seem to pick up any pace. There seem to be a lot of flash backs or flash forwards, whatever way you look at it, but that does not add to the story at all, it actually makes it complicated, slowing down the actual story. They should have stuck to the theme and kept it chronological. I was so bored I actually stopped watching it, I never made it to the end, and I'll never will. If you really are in to the theme of the movie and you want to see the story and are not phased by possible boredom, go see it, it's well played. If it's not fully your cup of tea but you have an open mind, try it, make up your mind yourself. 		<shortDescription>Could have been a lot better.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>When I read Kathryn Stockett's novel, The Help, it took me less than three days to read cover to cover. I fell in love with every character and imagined that, as a film, it would be stunning, emotive and compelling. Unfortunately, this adaptation didn't live up to my expectations.While Viola Davis and Jessica Chastain in particular, were both cast perfectly for their roles, I feel that a lot of the characters lacked individuality and spark. Some lovely characters (like Lou Anne) were erased all together, which disappointed me. Other characters, like Elizabeth Leefolt and Elaine Stein were cast incorrectly and did not live up to their full potential, I believe.One commendable thing that The Help does, is that it tries to involve as much of the original story as possible. However, the book itself is so intricately woven with numerous stories, I feel that a lot of the poignant story lines from the book were not portrayed very well and appeared patchy in the film. Skeeter and Stuart's relationship, Celia's ongoing struggle with her change in class, and Mae Mobely's alone time with Aibeleen, for example. Another thing is Skeeter's mother's illness. In the book, her deteriorating health acts as an extended metaphor for the anonymously published book, and the struggles faced through it, but none of this was shown in the film, despite the fact that it could have worked very well.I don't feel that drama is created particularly well, (the whole involvement of Constantine came across as a bit 'naff' unlike the book, where it was incredibly moving). However, comedic scenes are the film's strongest point in my eyes. Minnie's 'Terrible Awful' occurrence and Celia Foote's portrayal in particular, were very funny, and evoked genuine laugh out loud moments.All in all, however, I am sad to say that, despite some brilliant acting, The Help failed to live up to it's potential, appearing mismatched and vague. I feel that it would have developed better had it been created as a TV series. A slightly above average film, but still rather disappointing.</fullReview>

Viola Davis produces an Oscar-worthy performance, she had everything down to perfection: her accent, her mannerisms, she was the character. The character of Minny, though stereotyped, was a brilliant representation of black women in fear and under the thumb of not only their employers but their husbands, something that was so true at the time. Octavia Spencer's portrayal is hilarious and heartbreaking and the arc that was portrayed was magnificent. Jessica Chastain was another actress who shone in this film, her character had my eyes streaming and my heart breaking. The only performance that falls short is Emma Stone's. Although convincing, at times she's awkward and her accent is flawed. Nonetheless, she gave a very satisfying performance. However, to me, there is one scene that is embedded into my mind. The scene in which Yule Mae asks for a loan from Hilly, the performance of Aunjanue Ellis is convincing and truly heartbreaking as you see the hope in her eyes shattered by the cruel employer.The visuals are something else that make the film even more refined. The colours are gorgeous and the quality of the film making was impressive.This film, although fictional, is a true depiction of life for a maid. There are two words that I'm in danger of overusing in describing this film, but the content and substance is on par with the standard of the film making, ultimately making an incredible, heartbreaking film. I am singing this film's praise because there are not many films like this nowadays, films that make you think, make you feel, make you angry. The Help is a gem, that should be shown to everyone.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>Skeeter (Emma Stone) decides to write a book about the life of maids in the old south in Mississippi in the late 1950s. I didn't want to take this movie out because I knew what I would see and hear within. I was not wrong, and I am sure that things were much worse than I imagined and much worse than what is depicted in here. So keep that in mind. This was a very hard time for black maids serving white families especially caring for their daughter babies, who grew up to be like their mothers who instilled prejudice and southern ways in their children. Of course, not all white families treated their maids badly,but this story is not about them. Don't let anyone tell you that this was not a true story. It is that and more. It is inspired by true events and handed-down stories by these maids and what is seen is only a small tip of a large iceberg. You come away with the question: how can people treat people like this? But, this is part of our history such as it was. The acting performances by all the cast deserve Oscars. The way this was directed and performed was like we were eavesdropping into the lives of these maids. Nothing was overdone. Well, the pie incident was and I was shocked that it was included in here. Not good. That was an attempt for cheap laughs in a story that didn't see much humor. The standouts were Minny (Octavia Spencer) and Abileen (Viola Davis) and this was basically their story. You liked them and hoped the best for them especially when things didn't go good and you found yourself reaching for that Kleenex box time and time again. When you looked into their eyes you saw, without question, a sense of hopelessness within their lives. Minny says "we are living in hell, our children too. Trapped." You might need two boxes of Kleenex tissues. This is a powerful story and will, perhaps, turn out to become a classic and as such, it should be required viewing in all high schools throughout the land as most children now have no clue to this part of our history. The story aside, I was impressed with the vivid photography throughout, the colors of the maids' uniforms, numerous 1950s cars, and a lack of hard language usually found in today's movies. The Help was no exception for me. Just a few of the disappointments: The Hilly character in the book was an over-weight, scheming, and domineering woman. The Skeeter character was not developed well and much was left out of the film about the effect her actions had on her life. And the most puzzling thing for me at the ending was the scene where Constantine's daughter appears at Skeeter's home. The real impact of this in the book was that the daughter's skin color was that of a white person, and that was considered by some at the time to be quite shameful and embarrassing and reason enough to fire Constantine. I wonder how anyone watching the film could be able to get that impression.I realize that film and literature are different and I'm glad that most viewers enjoyed the film for what it was, but I still was disappointed that the film did not have the richness and drama of the the book. I wonder what a really talented director could have done with the story. Part of the problem for me, I suppose, was in seeing the movie too soon after reading the book. 		<date>2012-01-02</date>
		<username>classicalsteve</username>
		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>The Untold Story of African-American Maids Raising White Children in the South: The Best Film on the Subject Since "The Long Walk Home"</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>In American historic culture, we often think of race relations in terms of the civil rights protest marches of the 1960's and the work of Medgar Evers and Martin Luther King Jr. Behind the scenes and for many decades prior to the legislation signed by President Johnson in 1964, African-Americans and Southern Whites interacted within a societal infrastructure which was not often discussed. African-American hired "help" often raised the white children of southern aristocratic society. Eugenia "Skeeter" Phelan is a young white woman raised by one of these African-Americans, Constantine Bates, whom she regards as the person who raised her. She decides to document the experiences of the maids when she finds out Constantine was fired by her family without her knowledge.Aibileen Clark, played by Viola Davis in an Academy Award-caliber performance, is the first maid solicited by Skeeter. At first reluctant to share her thoughts and experiences, Clark eventually decides to reveal her story. Others follow, including Minny Jackson (Octavia Spencer) who was once in the employ of Hilly Holbrook, the most racist and insensitive of the upstart white women. As Skeeter hears their stories, she finds a recurring theme of negative attitudes exhibited by the white women toward the Help, ranging from disrespect and discourtesy to racist contempt. At one point, Hilly tries to have legislation passed to require all households with hired help to have separate bathrooms for African-Americans.Ironically, Celia Foote, the one white woman who was formerly among the "poor white trash" treats her new employee Minnie with respect and decency. But because of her background, Celia is shunned from the women's groups. At one point, she tries to visit the local women's bridge club, but the members hide inside the house, pretending they're not there. Eventually, she learns the hard way that prejudice can cut in either direction. Her employee Minnie becomes more than just the Help but a kind of mentor.Despite its marketing, "the Help" is not a comedy-drama. It really is a rather sobering look at stratified culture in Jackson, Mississippi in the early 1960's during the civil rights protests. The aristocratic mothers have hired African-Americans to care for their children and houses, but show little appreciation toward the people who are part of their lives. In several instances these children know the Help better than their white mothers. In a very poignant scene, a local Ladies Club made up of many of the young mothers has taken up a fund-raiser to help starving children in Africa. But they seem oblivious to the people in their own community who might benefit from their work. Even simply offering a kind word appears to be outside of their modus operandi. I guess the point is that no one in these families believes it's necessary, and so these prejudicial attitudes are passed from generation to generation. Unfortunately, when the white children become adults they don't necessarily have the same affection for those who raised them.No question, one of the best films of 2011, with outstanding performances by all the leads and supporting cast. I believe there will be several Academy Award Nominations. While Emma Stone as Skeeter and Emma Howard as Hilly deserve high marks, Viola Davis and Cicely Tyson steal the show. Both exhibit very different persona's when they are in the midst of whites than when they are at home. This may be the best film of this subject since "the Long Walk Home".</fullReview>
	</review>

		<fullReview>Reminiscent of "Fried Green Tomatoes" in the way it feels, "The Help", directed by Tate Taylor is a strong drama that "tugs at your heart- strings" effectively. The developing story keeps your interest piqued for the majority of a hefty runtime (137 min) which is testament to the sharpness of the script and the quality of acting. Indeed, Taylor puts together one of the best movies of 2011 filled with the right touches of humor. See it for the acting, story, and drama. Strongly recommended</fullReview>

		<username>theauntsavant</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Screen Adaptation Stays True to Revealing Exposé of the Help</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Debut author Kathryn Stockett's New York Times bestseller was transformed into one of the most talked about screen adaptations at the time of its release. Her period-based novel is a revealing exposé on the treatment of southern black maids in Jackson, Mississippi in the racially divisive culture of the 1960's?an era she grew up in, and like many others, her family employed domestic help. Both poignant and eye-opening, the plot uncovers an aspect of fairly recent history you won't find in any textbook. The screenplay stays true to the book with a young, white liberal-minded protagonist woman writer of an anonymously veiled tell-all about the community's local society-set. This theme-driven drama presents strong depictions by members of the virtually all-female cast, comprised of Academy Award nominated Viola Davis (Aibileen) and Octavia Spencer (Minny) as the first maids to courageously tell their experiences of working for the close-knit womenfolk to Skeeter, played by Emma Stone. A budding journalist, Stone's character is distressed to learn that even though they clean, cook, serve and tend to their children; the maids are not allowed to use the household bathrooms. She initiates a secret writing project in which she compiles personal accounts from their perspectives on the relationships between the hired help and those they may have worked years for. Davis is the tolerant maid, who suffers a heartbreaking loss. Spencer is a standout as the brash and sassy scene-stealer. Bryce Dallas Howard, daughter of Ron Howard, gives a brilliant portrayal of unfeeling antagonistic housewife Hilly, whom you'll love to hate. A reflective look into the past highlights the harsh realities of the times when bigotry ruled the Jim Crow South; the Help will make you laugh and cry. Although there is a gross factor alert that some may find distasteful. The inevitable fallout occurs after the book's release.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2011-08-22</date>
		<username>Likes_Ninjas90</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>Several of the performances make The Help an endearing look at a dark chapter in American history</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>This is an adaptation of Kathryn Stockett's bestselling novel, set in Jackson, Mississippi, during the 1960s. The film follows three different women. The first is Skeeter (Emma Stone), a young woman hired to write a column about housecleaning. She's also wondering what happened to her maid Constantine, who helped raise her, but has now disappeared. The second is Aibileen (Viola Davis) and Skeeter asks her for advice with her column. She's a maid for Elizabeth (Ahna O'Reilly) a snobby woman who can barely be bothered looking after her own children. The third woman is Minny (Octavia Spencer) and she finds work as a maid for Celia (Jessica Chastain), who lives out of town. Celia doesn't want her husband to know that she can't cook or clean so Minny has to help secretly. Bring these women together is Skeeter's plan to write about how African American maids are treated by their white employers. She's upset that one of her friends Hilly (Bryce Dallas Howard) is looking to enforce a rule that will force the 'help' to have separate bathrooms from their white employers. But Skeeter finds the maids reluctant to share their experiences because of the dangerous political climate in Jackson. Tate Taylor's second feature film The Help is an absorbing period drama, regardless of whether you have read Kathryn Stockett's novel. I have read the novel and the essence of the story is retained. It's a moving story that suggests that change is found in people of all ages, generations and races. It believes in the power of literature to inform people and to change the world. And there are ideas of class and the importance of public perception in this time period too. There are no easy divisions in either the novel or the film. Not all the white's are selfish or simply racist. They're willing to alienate their own people too. We see the women's deliberate exclusion of Celia and also learn that Hilly put her own mother (Sissy Spacek) in a nursing home. But nor are all the African American's innocent either. They make poor choices too. An ongoing thread is that after Minny was fired she decided to give Hilly a booby trapped cake, which she later regrets. And off camera it's apparent that Minny is physically abused at home. Though many scenes from the novel have been omitted entirely, it's a very solid adaptation. The film is beautiful in recreating the period, but never glossy. Despite being nearly two and a half hours long the film doesn't linger either. It's well paced and the images resonate with us. One of the most memorable is seeing the 'help' walk out of their homes of a morning, dressed in their uniforms. We see Minny giving instructions to her daughter who is now part of the 'help'. That subtly reminds us that these women weren't just employed for one lifetime. What's missing from the film are the darker elements of the novel. In the novel there's a broader overview of the violent atrocities around Mississippi that's absent here. It is indeed a compromise but more for business reasons rather than a political agenda. This is a mainstream film that has already flourished at the US box office. If the film was uglier less people might have seen it at all. But then it might have been more honest too. It's a great example of the dilemmas faced by the studio system. Instead, the fear and anxiety of the period comes through many of the performances. Almost all of the women in this film are superb. Emma Stone is extremely lively and funny as the oddball Skeeter. But she smartly replaces a lot of her flamboyance and sarcasm from Easy A (2010) with a greater sense of vulnerability. Some of her expressions when revelations about her mother are announced are extremely touching. But Viola Davis is the one almost certain to be nominated for Best Actress early next year. She has the bravest face in the film and yet underneath her internal conflict is so visible and moving. Her performance, particularly when she talks about her son, is painfully genuine. Spencer and Howard are charismatic women too. The latter contrasts the fake smiles of parties and charity dinners with ice-cold looks of suspicion perfectly. The only weak link is, I think, Chastain. I was expecting a quieter performance, closer to her excellent work in The Tree of Life (2011). She's extremely colourful here when her character in the novel is physically deteriorating and spends a lot of time alone in her room. Granted, that would be a lot harder to film on screen. The film might not have the grit of an independent picture like Precious (2009) but several of the performances make The Help an endearing look at a dark chapter in American history.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2011-08-21</date>
		<username>Jeff Heimbrock (heimbrockj@gmail.com)</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>The Help: A Pure Triumph, Through and Through</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Little known director Tate Taylor was certainly lucky to have such a pal as Kathryn Stockett. As the new girl in town heralding from Jackson, Mississippi, Stockett had a lot of trouble getting her first book published, The Help, based largely on the controversial topic that she was a white writer telling a story from a black woman's perspective. How shocking, how risqué?wait it is 2011, right? Are there such things as artistic boundaries anymore? Maybe for Taylor, whose film version of his friend's runaway hit novel, is stealing the hearts of America right before our eyes. And maybe he played it a bit safe, but it's a rare safeness that is hardly ever done well. Lucky, again, for Mr. Taylor, his result is amazing.The Help is about a woman trying to rev up a career as a writer, but lacks the connections, and frankly, the story to go on. Seeing the horrid mistreatment and racism going on in Jackson, Skeeter (Emma Stone) decides to write something that might help change all that. Her gentle motivation turns into passion and she begins to interview maids of her white- society-snob friends, and really gets onto something. Such a formulaic plot that we've seen before, but The Help does it with such humor and heart that it's spectacular. The filmmaking isn't innovative in any unique way, there is nothing really gritty or challenging about the racist terror that was in fact happening at the time, but I doubt the film would have benefited about that. It is not the story of a civil rights epic, but instead takes us to a more quiet advocacy, one that seems possible, and true, and yet altogether the most inspired. Whoever cast this film should get a raise. Emma Stone turns in a great performance, as usual, though her every-day-girl vibe doesn't allow her to stand out in this film, full of such distinct and memorable characters. Bryce Dallas Howard, as the main white antagonist Hilly Holbrook, is viciously evil and eats up every bit of this film with a force that makes us feel Howard knows the type of bigotry and hates that it still goes on; her character is written very one- sided, but she understands the inauthenticity and thoughtlessness of those women's racism, and plays it without fear. Jessica Chastain, hot off her dazzling understated performance in The Tree of Life, is so damn charming as the naive Celia, who knows about as much about how to treat African Americans in the south as she does to cook a pie, which is to say, not much. She is kind, funny, genuine, and it's hard not to love her. The real scene-stealers here are the undeniably spectacular performances from the two central maid characters: Viola Davis as Aibileen and Octavia Spencer as Minny. Both have strong dramatic moments, especially Davis, and Spencer has you laughing so damn hard you almost forget all the horrible things her character has to go through. It shows strength in the actress, and it shows strength in the character. Davis is a marvel; passive when she needs to be and then in full bloom as this beautiful and inspiring character when Aibileen can truly be herself around Minny or Skeeter. Through such a formulaic story and filmmaking techniques, it could have been a much different, mediocre film. But the incredible cast along with Taylor (with Stockett's help no doubt) make it feel authentic and as warm as a cherry pie from Jackson, Mississippi. And, damn, it tastes so good. A-</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>A charming, yet harrowing tale</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Set in Mississippi during the 1960s, Skeeter (Emma Stone) is a southern society girl who returns from college determined to become a writer, but turns her friends' lives and a Mississippi town turns upside down when she decides to interview the black women who have spent their lives taking care of prominent southern families and showcase their hardships to the world. Aibileen (Viola Davis), Skeeter's best friend's housekeeper, is the first to open up to the dismay of her friends in the tight-knit black community. Despite Skeeter's life-long friendships hanging in the balance, she and Aibileen continue their collaboration and soon more women come forward to tell their stories and as it turns out, they have a lot to say. Along the way, unlikely friendships are forged and a new sisterhood emerges, but not before everyone in town has a thing or two to say themselves when they become unwittingly and unwillingly caught up in the changing times.This is yet another Hollywood output where the story of the minorities is told through a morally righteous white person, but this time its done really well. Skeeter basically disappears after setting the movie up and only appears here and there when the story requires her and she leaves the floor for the others to shine. Institutional racism is fascinating and disturbing to watch, just like the sexism of the modern world which still exists in most countries. Hilly Holbrook (Bryce Dallas Howard), the antagonist in our tale, thinks of herself as a progressive thinker among her peers and yet her actions suggest otherwise. Her club's efforts to save the hungry children of Africa was ridiculously ironic and hilarious.The disparity, hardships and social status of the black people just because of the colour of their skin back then isn't exploited to the maximum. The critiques hurled at this movie for not showing it is simply laughable. This is just a story where none of the women shown on screen got raped or abused, though there are various viewpoints to the term, by their white masters and one should respect the story for it and not judge based on what didn't happen to these characters. At least, this is not a falsification of history like the supposed true story of Argo. Our critics and society are unfairly critical of female-oriented and minority based movies. Nothing is more telling than the hate bashing of the Twilight movies, a simple tale told through the eyes of a girl and her love for a supernatural being.I digress. The Help has a good story at its core, but its the strong performances by all the actors which is a sight to behold. Octavia Spencer won an Oscar for her efforts, though I do feel that her pie thing, though quite funny, was done in a slight distaste unbecoming of the movie, and Viola Davis was overlooked in my opinion. But it was Jessica Chastain, whom I liked in Lawless and Mama, who surprisingly impressed the hell out of me and was nominated for an Oscar for her role here. I was thinking that she'd just be another ditzy, suburban blonde, housewife/bombshell who I'd probably hate, but there was so much more to the woman and she made me empathize with her plight against all odds. She is definitely my new favourite actress. Emma Stone was infectious in her portrayal of Skeeter and the way her character's arc ended was realistic and sad. A charming, yet harrowing tale which doesn't overplay the aspect of racial discrimination to high levels.7.5/10</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Gets So Much Right and So Much Wrong....</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I'll give "The Help" 10 stars for the Women. Viola Davis, Octavia Spencer, Jessica Chastain, Cicely Tyson (playing old, old, old yet again) are thrilling to watch. When Emma Stone's Skeeter finally gets Viola Davis' Aibileen to talk to her for a book she's writing about the experiences of black women who serve as housemaids and nannies in assorted white households in Jackson Mississippi circa 1963, Aibileen very tentatively begins by relating a story of the first child she took care of as a 14 year old in 1925. The movie could have done with a lot more of the real stories about these women's lives but we only get bits and pieces. We really don't get to know a whole lot about them and that's a shame. We might think we get more but that is because these actresses are so damn good that we fill in the gaps ourselves.I'll give the "The Help" one star for what is gets wrong and it's not the first to do so. Coincidentally (or maybe not) Sissy Spacek (a bit horrifying here in an old lady role. She's only 62!) played this role once in a movie called "The Long Walk Home". I assume that the money behind this film felt that in order to get white audiences interested, they had to keep this legacy going. While I think it's OK to have this kind of role, the film makers once again my the role pretty much the central character but they try and circumvent this by having Viola Davis' Aibileen narrate the picture (though this narration seems to be absent throughout long stretches of the film). It's insulting to the main characters in the film ("The Help" of the film's title) and diminishes from some of the meaning the film has. The rest of the white cast ranges from barely adequate/watchable (Spacek) to eye rolling caricature (Bryce Dallas Howard's Hilly Holbrook). The least said about Howard's character the better.I'll recommend "The Help" based solely on the African-American actresses (and Chastain as the only white character with some depth of feeling that seemed genuine). The rest is filler that we've seen before and can do without.</fullReview>

		<username>Jenny</username>
		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>You is kind, You is smart, You is important.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>You is kind. You is smart. You is important.So says black maid Abilene to Mae Mobley, the small daughter of her wealthy white employers. As the little girl repeats the words back her one can't help but wonder if Abilene has ever had them said to her. Living in a world where a black man inhabits the White House is difficult to imagine a time when the same man could be arrested for refusing to give up his seat on the bus for a white person.Set in Jacksonville, Mississippi in the 1960s this funny and poignant film, based the book by Kathryn Stockett, depicts a time, not so long ago, when a black maid couldn't use the same bathroom as her white employers, when black women raised white women's children but could not be trusted not to steal the silver and when telling the truth about your life as 'the help' was akin to signing your own death warrant.Into this world of prejudice and secrecy comes Eugenia 'Skeeter' Phelan a recent college graduate with dreams of becoming a journalist. Skeeter has returned home to find that her beloved maid Constantine, who raised her, has seemingly left to go and live with her family in another state. On top of being devastated and confused by this Skeeter is also trying to pave a career for herself in New York. To do so she must convince the editor that she is a good writer. With this mind she happens upon a radical and potentially dangerous idea; to tell the story of the black maids of Jacksonville, from their point of view, enlisting Abilene, and her best friend Minnie, maid to the cruel Hilly to help her.The three leads are perfectly cast. Viola Davis brings a quite sadness, tenderness and hidden strength to Abilene, Octavia Spencer perfectly portrays Minnie's brashness and loud mouth while also bringing out her caring side and vulnerability and Emma Stone shines as the bright and witty Skeeter, depicting perfectly her determination to change things and her confusion and sadness over the loss of her maid.There are so many outstanding performances among the supporting cast in this film it seems unfair to single anyone out. However credit must be given where it is due and there are three performances' which really stuck with me. Bryce Dallas Howard, who I also thought of as decent but nothing special, really shines in the role of Hilly, the ultimate society queen bee and an out and out racist to boot. You really find yourself disgusted with her actions and attitude. Also a stand-out is Jessica Chastain as Ceila, town out cast and apparent man eater whose Marilyn Monrone-like facade, and pretty rose bushes, hide a tragic secret.And while she has only three or four scenes, Cicely Tyson's scenes as Constantine, Skeeter's, much loved maid, are among the most touching and poignant in the film. Her final scene, in which we learn the true nature of her leaving the Phelan home, would break the hardest of hearts. Honourable mentions to Sissy Spacek as Hilly's slightly mad mother and Allison Janney as Skeeter's mother, a woman struggling with both a serious illness and her actions regarding Constantine.The score and cinematography are amazing but it is the performances that make this film. It is touching and warm, poignant and sad, funny and uplifting. It is also important as a commentary on the dangerous judging people based on physical appearance and deals well with it's serious subject matter, allow the message to come across clearly without being too depressing or hard hitting.All in all this is a must-see movie and one for the Oscars.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>A very sincere, hilarious, and emotionally powerful movie lies within The Help.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I took the time recently to see a wonderful ensemble drama called The Help, a film based on the novel by Kathryn Stockett.The film centers around two main story lines in Jackson, Mississippi in the 1960's: First of which are the African-American housemaids working for white families. This includes Aibileen (Played by Viola Davis), a woman whose spent her whole life as a maid, not to mention having lost her only son, but still stays true to her beliefs.The other main narrative is that of Eugenia Phelan, aka. Skeeter (Played by Emma Stone), a woman recently hired by the local Jackson newspaper, who wants to have interviews from the point of view of the housemaids, even though they could face jail time for doing so. Never the less, Aibileen accepts, and Skeeter's project starts to form.Some would probably accuse The Help's screenplay of feeling emotionally forced, but what writer/director Tate Taylor constructs is actually a more honest, emotionally involving project. The themes of racial segregation give the film a dramatic edge, but it's also brimming with irresistible humor. What could have been a sappy melodrama actually feels more sincere.Same goes for the cast, who could have been butchered if not for casting directors Kerry Barden and Paul Schnee just nailing it. It's hard to pick a standout, but it's worth noting the individuals for their work.Jessica Chastain performs with equal hilarity and grace. Viola Davis, the closest thing to a standout, gives off understated, but powerful emotion. Bryce Dallas Howard, normally a very sweet soul, is evil enough to get your nostrils flaring. Allison Janney gives off a sarcastic, but also sympathetic vibe. Sissy Spacek tickles in her small supporting role. Octavia Spencer excels with a no bull crap attitude. And Emma Stone, with her charm and believability, works wonders as Skeeter.I must also make note of the technical crew of the film. Sharen Davis' costume define an extra depth of personality, and Thomas Newman supplies another emotionally potent orchestral score. Oh, and I almost forgot about editor Hughes Winborne. At a little over two hours and fifteen minutes long, the movie just flies by.Forgive me if I overrate, but The Help was too good for me to ignore. I give it ***1/2 out of ****</fullReview>

		<username>Movie_Muse_Reviews</username>
		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>Amazing female ensemble delivers perfect mixture of humor and drama</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Late-summer Civil Rights dramas don't come around much. In fact, late-summer dramas don't come around much period, but "The Help" has just the right pinches of humor and bright colors to keep it from becoming a weighty affair more suited for the winter awards contenders. Don't assume, however, that come the turn of seasons that anyone will have forgotten "The Help" come awards time.Tate Taylor adapts and directs Kathryn Stockett's best-selling novel about African-American maids living in Jackson, Miss. in the Jim Crow era. Viola Davis plays Aibileen, a woman who has been raising the children of wealthy Southern white families for decades. When college grad Eugenia "Skeeter" Phelan (Emma Stone) returns home with aspirations of becoming a journalist, she tries to convince Aibileen and fellow maid Minnie (Octavia Spencer) to tell their stories in hopes of getting their perspective heard. Although the women fear their identities being discovered, the prejudicial attitudes of Miss Hilly Holbrook (Bryce Dallas Howard) and her initiative to force homes with maids to provide a separate bathroom for The Help provokes them to courageously speak up."The Help" puts out a massive ensemble effort glowing with talent. Never has a female- dominated cast of this size given such strong performances across the board. Aibileen provides the narrative voice, but Skeeter, Minnie and others each get their stories told in full depth and the supporting roles from the likes of Allison Janney as Skeeter's mother and Sissy Spacek as Hilly's mother are priceless.If a standout has to be chosen for Oscar consideration, it has to be Davis. There's that constant look in her eyes suggesting that she's always carrying every single thing her character has ever been through into every scene. She's penetratingly real; she is everyone's mother. She also brings the film's wide range of emotions, though the comedy would be Spencer's forte.Spencer, a long-time small-role character actor, follows Taylor from his first feature-length film "Pretty Ugly People." She makes the film a true delight with a hold-nothing-back attitude. Minnie embodies some racial stereotypes, but the script gives her such incredible depth that we associate those things with who she truly is, not as a vehicle for humor.Perhaps ? oddly enough ? too many characters feel multi-dimensional and "The Help" lacks a true focus for a film running nearly two and a half hours, but the acting is so sharp at all levels that it never feels boring or hollow. The characters and the many situations and problems they deal with feel unmistakably real, namely because those problems break the mold for Southern Civil Rights drama.The film avoids being another example of how terrible things once were and how poorly blacks were treated. It asks us to feel sorry for everyone, not just the African-American characters and scratch our heads at how life was ever like that anywhere in this country. Ultimately, "The Help" tells a story of courage in one of its unlikeliest forms ? telling the truth, making sure all side are heard. It uses such a diverse tool set of characters to get that point across.Taylor's much more concerned about working with these actresses to create compelling portraits of characters and relationships in a place and time full of unique issues than he is with creating gripping drama. Considering the film is no less poignant and maybe all the more likable for it, he made the right choice.~Steven CVisit my site at http://moviemusereviews.com</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2011-08-20</date>
		<username>Bob Pr.</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>If you loved the book, you'll love the movie.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Set in Jackson, Mississippi, in the early '60s, "Skeeter" (Emma Stone) is a recent college graduate aspiring to be a writer or novelist. Having been raised by a Negro nanny whom she adored, she decides to interview other local Negroes who serve as maids or nannies to white families in the area, and write a book about their experiences. The film shows some of the barriers between whites and blacks; Skeeter's efforts help begin to surmount some of those.I spent a year in Mississippi in the early '60s and, IMO, the film's vision of the (white) Junior League of that era is slightly a caricature--but fairly accurate in flavor. What is quite inaccurate is having a Jr. Leaguer in Skeeter's fact-finding role at that time, or having blacks daring to support Skeeter to the extent they're shown doing. It's equally inaccurate to portray racist whites as hateful; in that era, many white people with otherwise admirable qualities were racists.In a sense, the movie has a fairy tale quality with time traveling mixed in (i.e., as if today's sensibilities travel back to examine the '60s Mississippi scene, inhabit Skeeter and a few blacks, and then begin taking corrective action through them). It's an "ultra light civil rights" version of Mississippi (&amp; similar parts of the South), one that ignores the overlapping memberships of community leaders in White Citizen Councils, police in the Ku Klux Klan, and all those, and many, many others, staunch members in many white churches.But the film's acting was great: Davis, Spencer, Howard, Chastain, &amp; Stone make this fairy tale come alive. It's primarily about relationships between women (and women DO seem to enjoy the movie very much). IMDb's demographic ratings consistently reflect that females, whatever their ages, rate the film about 10% higher than males; it seems much more appealing to women--likewise, for my 2 female companions at the movie, it was a 10 of 10.It's fairly safe to say: if you loved the book, you'll love this movie.</fullReview>
	</review>
